# Aff -- Benefit Sharing - DGHL

### Notes

Thank you to Ava, Max, Ishaan, Darin, Niko, Victoria, Lucy, and Austin
for their work on this file.

Benefit sharing is when corporations cooperate with Native Alaskans when
doing mineral extraction on their land.

Status quo benefit sharing operates under 3 regimes:

a)  Paternalistic: Companies operate and own the land while providing
    for Native Alaskans. Native Alaskans get a lump sum dividend.

b)  Company-driven model where corporations voluntarily offer benefits
    (e.g., jobs, revenue-sharing, social investments) to communities in
    exchange for project approval or goodwill.

c)  Co-Management: Native Alaskans co-manage and co-design the facility.

The AFF mandates new mineral resource extraction under the third model.
The BLM does the plan in cooperation with the BIA and the US Geological
Survey.

# 1AC

## 1AC

### Advantage\-\--1AC

#### The advantage is [COOPERATION]{.underline}:

#### Benefit sharing agreements regarding mineral extraction in the Arctic strategically disadvantage Native Alaskans now.

5\. Discussion: Considering Advantages and Disadvantages of Benefit
Sharing Regimes

This paper developed the typology of various [benefit sharing regimes
[in]{.mark} the Arctic]{.underline} and illustrated them with
[**examples** from Russia, **[Alaska]{.mark}**, and Canada.]{.underline}
In the cases of the NAO, YNAO, and KMAO regions of Russia we see
path-dependent practices of benefit sharing at the regional level vested
in the principles of social infrastructure investment, compensation, and
charity and implemented through a largely paternalistic mode
\[3,16,17\]. [In all these regions, the [most **common
practice**]{.mark} [is]{.mark} to conduct socio-economic
[agreements]{.mark} [between]{.mark} [companies]{.mark} [and]{.mark} the
regional governor. [Regional authorities]{.mark}, with **little input**
from indigenous and local communities, [act as]{.mark} the **[main
actors]{.mark}** in decision-making in respect to social investment and
industrial activity,]{.underline} thus reproducing Soviet and
post-Soviet paternalistic experiences \[17,30,34\].
[**[Indigenous]{.mark}** and local **peoples [participation]{.mark}** in
the decision-making processes **[is minimal]{.mark}**. Therefore, the
benefit sharing regime at the regional level is characterized by
insufficient procedural and distributive equities]{.underline} \[5\],
[strong path-dependency, extractivism and overreliance on semi-formal
and trickle-down mechanisms.]{.underline}

If we look at the local scale and direct interactions between companies
and indigenous peoples in the Russian Arctic, we observe a continuum of
regimes that span from strictly paternalistic to CCSR mode-based
arrangements. It is important to point out that benefit sharing regimes
differ considerably among Russian territories. YNAO represents an
example of a regime where local agreements are setup via the regional
and local governments, and no separate agreements with indigenous
communities are negotiated, except for state-owned reindeer herding
enterprises. In NAO we observed a move towards CCSR due to the switch to
the new federal methodology for calculating the compensation for damage,
which partly substituted socio-economic agreements between companies and
indigenous peoples. The new arrangement gave more leverage, funds and
spending freedom to reindeer herders. However, we concur with the
literature \[17,29\] that in Russia, CCSR-based benefit sharing regimes
echo the Soviet experiences. They are characterized by the companies'
paternalistic attitudes towards the indigenous communities and desire to
retain the decision-making power in respect to benefits. This is often
done by applying the charity principle to benefit distribution and
reliance on semi-formal arrangements, in which communities are typically
at the mercy of an extractive company. Direct benefit sharing
arrangements between companies and indigenous peoples in NAO and KMAO
are determined by the regional legislation. In NAO companies are obliged
to compensate for expropriated and/or damaged agricultural land leased
by reindeer herding enterprises. In KMAO, companies are required to
compensate for building infrastructure and damaging the TTNU lands.

[Benefit sharing regimes]{.underline} based on both paternalism and CCSR
[are characterized by the **limited involvement** of indigenous peoples
in decision-making processes. During the construction phase of large oil
infrastructure,]{.underline} public hearings and [**[environmental
impact assessment]{.mark}** proceedings [are organized]{.mark} in larger
settlements [with **limited participation** of nomadic]{.mark} reindeer
[herders]{.mark}.]{.underline} The main decisions on social investments
are made via semi-formal negotiations between the company and the
authorities. Money is allocated for building social infrastructure,
purchasing goods and providing transport for indigenous peoples.
[[Indigenous]{.mark} peoples own [initiatives receive **little
attention**]{.mark}. There are no mechanisms developed for monitoring
public opinion and requests from the indigenous and other local
residents.]{.underline}

In contrast, the benefit sharing regime in Sakhalin is guided by the
corporate policies of the international consortia conducting the oil
extraction, as well as by the standards of international financial
institutions. Both Sakhalin Energy and Exxon Neftegaz Limited
strategically involve the state actors in the tripartite partnerships in
order to avoid state pressure to force these companies to fill gaps in
the state budget and public services (which happens with the state-owned
extractive companies, such as Rosneft) \[37\]. In the case of Sakhalin,
the transnational actors brought in the international standards that are
reflected in benefit sharing arrangements. The peculiar feature of the
tripartite model is that the companies readily involve indigenous people
in making decisions about their own development. Sakhalin Energy is
granting indigenous representatives the power to allocate and distribute
funds without assistance from the company or the state. Both companies
enabled a procedure of filing complaints to the company. The information
about the results of grant competitions, the amount of money granted and
the beneficiaries are fully disclosed to the community. Social
investment involves supporting and promoting local initiatives \[3\].

Successful benefit-sharing practices can help companies to ensure a
long-term SLO and can enhance sustainability in resource-dependent
communities \[14,33,34\]. However, [the practices of interactions with
stakeholders differ in the investigated case studies.]{.underline} In
NAO and KMAO we observed the shift from arrangements based on the
financial aid (charity) principle to the compensation principle of
benefit sharing with the growing role of CCSR. [[This kind of
**benefit-sharing**]{.mark} may [enhance]{.mark} [corporate
efficiency]{.mark} for the **short-term**, [but does not]{.mark}
guarantee long-term SLO and does not [eliminate **risks of
conflicts**]{.mark}, especially in cases when compensations are small
and/or are not calculated properly.]{.underline} However, this does not
mean that the tripartite partnership model in Sakhalin is unequivocally
better for effective benefit-sharing among indigenous communities. The
partnership mode assumes that the communities are ready to comply with
the transparent rules, as well as the companies themselves. This also
assumes that the community is able to take responsibility for its
decisions. This, however, is not always true. For instance, in the case
of Sakhalin, the company delegated power to indigenous representatives
to distribute grants between households, which in turn entailed
conflicts. In the partnership mode, local initiatives and
entrepreneurship workshops are prioritized, however, not all training
programs prove to be efficient. In a variety of cases, investing
corporate funds by Russian companies in the construction of communities'
facilities turns out to be a more important instrument of social
investment \[34\].

The Alaskan and Canadian Arctic benefit sharing regimes differ
considerably from one another and from Russia. This can be explained by
differences in land ownership, as well as by diverging political and
economic contexts. In Russia the land belongs to the state and land with
mineral deposits is leased to the extractive companies. Benefits from
lease sales go to the state. In both Alaska and Canada, a significant
sum of money comes to indigenous communities via royalty payments. In
Russia when companies encroach upon land (TTNU or agricultural) leased
by indigenous reindeer herders, these companies pay (relatively minimal)
compensation for the loss of pastures. In Alaska in the framework of
ANCSA, and in Canada in the framework of CLCAs, land is owned by
indigenous peoples and a significant amount of royalties are paid to
indigenous communities when oil companies use the land. These royalty
payments are much greater than compensations in Russia. In both Alaska
and Canada, a significant effort was made to invest in indigenous
communities and build capacities by creating indigenous-run businesses.
Such benefit sharing mechanisms have largely not emerged in Russia. In
Alaska, Native corporations, are involved in oil drilling themselves,
and in Canada regional corporations invest and maintain a variety of
businesses. [In both **Alaska** and Canada,]{.underline} however, [there
are often [**tensions** between]{.mark} indigenous [traditional
activities]{.mark} and non-traditional businesses involvement
[with]{.mark} potentially [negative impacts]{.mark} of the expanding
wage economy [on traditional practices and cultural
vitality]{.mark}]{.underline} \[48,49\].

[Benefit sharing regimes based on the shareholder mode in
Alaska]{.underline} and beneficiary mode in Canada are relatively unique
for the Arctic and may present considerable interest to other world
regions, although they do [have]{.underline} their strengths and
**[weaknesses]{.underline}**. Conceptually, [each regime has
**characteristics** that support increasing the wealth of both
**individuals** and **communities**]{.underline}. In both cases the
indigenous peoples are empowered to make decisions on distribution of
benefits, however, [some of these [decisions]{.mark} may [fail to foster
sustainability]{.mark} in local communities.]{.underline} At the same
time, [in the North Slope of Alaska village [corporations create
**intergenerational** **inequalities**]{.mark} by denying the
"afterborns'" shares]{.underline} and in Canada the distribution of
payments to individuals may elevate alcohol and drug abuse and disrupt
social services.

The RMS involves fully compensatory money. Oil companies are involved
both in investments and charitable contributions. [In **Alaska**,
**all** paternalistic, CCSR, and partnership modes are
observed]{.underline}, in which the shareholder mode is dominant and
unique.

[Mechanisms of benefit-sharing arrangements **depend** on land
ownership]{.underline} (royalty payments), [existing legal,
institutional, and cultural environments]{.underline}, [as well as on
the company that operates in the area]{.underline}, such as
transnational corporations, native regional or native village
corporations.

Legislation, contracts and/or regulation requires mandated mechanisms.
[Under]{.underline} the [**paternalistic** mode,]{.underline} they
involve the distribution of taxes that companies pay to the NSB and
[royalties paid to the state.]{.underline} [Under]{.underline} the
[**CCSR** mode]{.underline}, they are [represented by community

Slope Foundation, [created by native corporations]{.underline} that
serve for channeling money from oil companies. [In]{.underline} a
[partnership mode]{.underline} [by fixed contracts]{.underline} [between
transnational oil companies and native corporations]{.underline}, these
contractual relationships simultaneously contribute to the shareholder
mode as money earned via contracts is distributed to shareholders. In
the shareholder mode, ANCSA legislation requires mandated sharing.

[Negotiated **benefits** **represent** a **large group** which can be
different under different modes]{.underline}. For example[,
**benefit-sharing** arrangements]{.underline} that were [negotiated as

policies]{.underline} and royalties paid in the framework of land use
agreements. Therefore, [**both** participatory and distributed equity
are **limited**]{.underline}. [Under]{.underline} the [**partnership**

**co-management**]{.underline} [and coordination between industrial
activities and subsistence or cultural activities]{.underline}, which
includes the flexibility of industrial activities depending on wildlife
migrations or events important for indigenous hunting. In case the

to accommodate their subsistence hunting activities.

[Semi-formal benefits involve]{.underline} [different]{.underline} kinds
of [goods]{.underline} and services [that communities get upon
request]{.underline} [from]{.underline} the [NSB, transnational oil
companies, or native corporations]{.underline}. We call this an "ask and
get" system. Sponsorship that the local community requests also belongs
to this category. Trickle-down benefits involve those coming from
industrial development as such, including employment, infrastructure the
company builds for its own operation and used by communities, increased
cash flow, local economic growth, and income growth.

Principles, modes, and mechanisms together provide a systematic
framework for analyzing benefit-sharing regime in Alaska \[**21**\]. To
understand how and to what extent benefit-sharing arrangements are
equitable in terms of participatory and distributive equity, we looked
at principles, modes, and mechanisms of delivery of monetary and
non-monetary support by particular actors (see **Table 1**).

**top--down** approach to service delivery]{.underline} \[**61**\].
[**Participatory equity** is **low** under the paternalistic
mode]{.underline}, despite distributive equity being high as communities
justly receive large sums of money. As a result, [communities became
totally dependent on **oil money**]{.underline}, and created and

#### Companies only engage in enough benefit-sharing agreements necessary to operate in indigenous lands

**Alubankudi 21**, \* Master's in Polar Law at the University of
Akureyri. (Thelma Sefakor Alubankudi, 06-01-2021, \"Sustainable
Development of Arctic Oil and Gas: Indigenous Peoples' Rights and
Benefit-Sharing.\", Icelandic E-Journal of Nordic & Mediterranean
Studies,
https://nome.unak.is/wordpress/volume-16-no-3-2021/sustainable-development-of-arctic-oil-and-gas-indigenous-peoples-rights-and-benefit-sharing/)
//ishi

Benefit-sharing can be described as a fair and equal distribution of the
monetary and non- monetary benefits produced by resource extraction
activities. Rewards include the allocation of taxes and royalties,
business, and equity ownership, employment creation, negotiated
arrangements, and community development (Wilson, 2019). Globally,
benefit-sharing offers mean that indigenous/local populations and
extractive industries cooperate peacefully to turn the resource "curse"
into a developmental advantage (Petrov & Tysiachniouk, 2019). [In remote
areas in the Arctic, oil and natural gas production offers growth
opportunities and also raises costs for residents, indigenous
communities, and cultures.]{.underline} It affects the economy's
survival and reduces the traditional resource utilization of land
(Tysiachniouk & Petrov, 2018). Benefit-sharing is a legal requirement
and a component of corporate social responsibility that can promote
sustainable development in the remote Arctic regions if adequately
structured. [In the Arctic, sustainable development can be defined as
development that enhances the well-being, health, and protection of
Arctic populations and inhabitants, while maintaining the institutions,
roles, and resources of ecosystems (Petrov & Tysiachniouk,
2019).]{.underline} On the other hand, Corporate Social Responsibility
(CSR) is a management principle in which organizations combine fiscal,
social, and environmental issues in their business practices and the
relationship with their stakeholder ("What Is CSR? \| UNIDO," n.d.).

[Sometimes, companies engage in corporate social responsibility
benefit-sharing schemes to satisfy investors and shareholders and to
meet the needs of local communities only to the degree required to
receive the 'social license' to operate (Tysiachniouk & Petrov,
2018).]{.underline} The commitment of an organization to localities
often takes the form of compensation or targeted investments. However,
the corporation holds the leadership role in the decision-making power
of benefit sharing, making its preference prevail in several ways over
community needs and desires (Petrov & Tysiachniouk, 2019).

Therefore, [a fragile Arctic environment is of concern because of the
adverse consequences of extractive practices, which are now turned into
industrial **"green movements," resulting in the invasion of indigenous
lands** (Wilson, 2020) and causes danger to their ways of life, such as
herding, fishing, and farming.]{.underline} The focus on indigenous
rights is on FPIC principles. FPIC may take many forms but is an
important sustainable development corporate governance framework. It is
a right set out for Indigenous peoples in international treaties and
declarations, especially ILO Convention 169 on Indigenous and Tribal
Peoples and the United Nations Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous
Peoples and some national legislation (Buxton & Wilson, 2013).

Therefore, the absence of Indigenous peoples' representation in policy
decisions on creating the extractive sector in their territories,
including decisions on the allocation of land for extractive industry
operations and the awarding of exploration licenses, threatens the
possibilities for fair development results. [It is advised that there
should be an informed decision to monitor the benefit-sharing scheme,
and total community control of benefit-sharing and management must exist
to eliminate any controversy. As a result, companies and the state must
collaborate with indigenous and other impacted populations to develop
local institutional capacity and human resources as part of
benefit-sharing obligations.]{.underline} This will ensure that the
policies that share benefits are transparent, sensitive, empowering, and
lead in a just and equitable way to Arctic populations' sustainable
development. Thus, corporate social responsibility and Triple Bottom
Line initiatives should be monitored and enforced in every Arctic
state's soft laws. Every mining and oil industry player operating in the
Arctic must report companies' financial, social, and environmental
performance over time by protecting businesses amid future uncertainty.

#### Oil spills are [widespread]{.underline} and will [only get worse]{.underline}. 

Alison **Kirsch 18**, Senior Energy Campaigns Analyst at the Sierra

[Alaska Native rights and]{.underline} Indigenous [sovereignty **cannot
be separated from**]{.underline} the problem of extreme **[oil and gas
production in Alaska]{.underline}**. Politicians and oil interests have
a long history of pushing legislation nullifying Alaska Native land
claims, especially those claims that stood in the way of oil and
pipeline development.

After being declared a state in 1958, [Alaska selected]{.underline} for
oil development tracts of [land on the North Slope]{.underline}, in an
area called Prudhoe Bay.1 [Without consultation]{.underline} and consent
of the local Inupiat village, but with approval of the U.S. Bureau of
Land Management, these [lands were transferred to the
state]{.underline}.2

In 1964, the state leased some of these tracts to oil companies and in
1968, oil was discovered at Prudhoe Bay.3 [The oil boom **forever
altered the ecosystem**]{.underline} and the life of the Inupiat people,
including [by contributing to climate change through the release of
carbon]{.underline} from oil sent from the North Slope to refineries.
This all led to the building of the 800-mile Trans-Alaska pipeline
across the state that further trampled over the rights of Inupiat
peoples and Alaska Native land rights at large.4

The Alaska Native Claims Settlement Act (ANCSA) was passed in 1971, over
opposition from the Inupiat.5 ANCSA took land from jurisdictional
control of Alaska Natives, allowing the petroleum industry and State of
Alaska to gain access to oil reserves.6

[Native Alaskans are]{.underline} once again [confronted with **oil and
gas expansion**]{.underline} under the Trump administration, [with
potential]{.underline} oil [drilling in the]{.underline} Alaska Outer
Continental Shelf ([OCS]{.underline}) [and opening]{.underline} of
[the]{.underline} Alaska National Wildlife Refuge ([ANWR]{.underline})
[to]{.underline} oil and gas [development]{.underline}.7

In April 2017, Trump signed an executive order lifting a ban on oil
exploration in the coastal seas of Alaska. [The U.S. government
is]{.underline} now [proposing]{.underline} 19 [offshore lease
sales]{.underline} in the Chukchi Sea, the Beaufort Sea, Cook Inlet, and
other areas.8 [**Any oil spills** in]{.underline} these [Arctic waters
would be **catastrophic**]{.underline} for bowhead whales, seals, polar
bears and other marine mammals, [and]{.underline} would [directly affect
the Alaska Natives' **subsistence culture and way of
life**]{.underline}.9 [Spills or drilling disasters]{.underline} would
[mean high-risk cleanup challenged by ice cover, subzero temperatures,
and harsh weather conditions]{.underline}. There is no proven way to
clean sea ice of oil from potential spills.10

The U.S. Republican tax overhaul law passed in December 2017 included a
provision requiring two oil and gas lease sales in the coastal plains
area of the ANWR over the next 10 years.11

ANWR is one of the world's most pristine and beautiful ecosystems, with
landscapes and wildlife that demand the strongest protection. The Refuge
is home to porcupine caribou, muskoxen, moose, Arctic fox, lynx, wolves,
and polar bears, brown bears and black bears, and is a stopping point
for nearly 200 species of migratory birds.12

For Alaska Natives, such as the Gwich'in Athabascan peoples, protecting
the Refuge and the caribou they depend on is a matter of human rights
and the collective right of Indigenous peoples -- as well as a critical
matter of survival.13 [In opposing Arctic oil drilling, they are
fighting for their rights]{.underline} and the rights of the porcupine
caribou, whose calving grounds are within the coastal plain of the
Refuge. The Gwich'in call these caribou calving grounds Iizhik Gwats'an
Gwnadaii Goodlit: "the sacred place where life begins." The Gwich'in
have followed the caribou herd across the lands of this protected Refuge
for thousands of years.14 They are rightfully concerned that seismic
exploration and drilling for oil in the Refuge will have disastrous
effects on the delicate web of life in these sacred lands. Oil and gas
expansion in ANWR would carve up the Refuge with roads and industrial
infrastructure, fragmenting otherwise pristine habitat and exposing the
fragile tundra and wildlife to toxic chemicals, oil spills, and gas
leaks.15

[Alaska Native Peoples and the fragile Arctic ecosystem have yet to
fully recover from]{.underline} the disastrous 1989 [Exxon
Valdez]{.underline} terminal oil spill. [It killed thousands of animals,
severely affecting the]{.underline} local [food chain, as well as the
food security and sovereignty of Alaska Natives]{.underline}.16 [Another
spill would have a **catastrophic effect** on]{.underline} what remains
of [Alaska's pristine ecosystem and the way of life of its
peoples]{.underline}.17

Moreover, expansion of [oil and gas]{.underline} development in Alaska
[will]{.underline} lead to an [increase]{.underline} in global
[greenhouse gas emissions. Climate change is already a stark
reality]{.underline} in the Alaska Arctic ecosystem. The average air
temperature in the Arctic is warming twice as fast as the global
average.18 Coastal villages are experiencing the melting of permafrost,
which in turn triggers huge seasonal surges in CO2 emissions and coastal
erosion.19 Ice cover in the coastal waters is decreasing, making it more
difficult to obtain the seafood that sustains local Indigenous
peoples.20 Inland, porcupine caribou migration routes are changing,
threatening Gwich'in hunters that rely on the caribou.21

Expansion of [oil and gas development in Alaska is an **assault against
the Alaska Natives** who are defending the territorial integrity of

generations. Bank [financing]{.underline} of the expansion of [extreme
fossil fuels in Alaska perpetuates a long history of violations of the
human rights and collective rights of the Indigenous peoples of
Alaska]{.underline}.

#### Oil spills are bad for the local ecosystem

2\. Impacts of oil spill on Arctic and Subarctic wildlife and human

[Spilled oil in Arctic or Subarctic waters does not remain stationary
but is carried by wind and currents]{.underline} (horizontally and
vertically), [often covering considerably large areas]{.underline}. [Oil
will likely reach shoreline regions or become trapped within sea ice,
affecting coastal wildlife and local communities (Fig. 2), which are
highly dependent on the sustenance and habitat of a healthy
ocean.]{.underline}

Fig. 2

Fig. 2. Impacts of spilled oil on wildlife and human in coastal regions
of Arctic and Subarctic areas.

2.1. Invertebrates

[Copepods of the genus Calanus, the key invertebrates in Arctic areas,
are the primary herbivores of marine ecosystems.]{.underline} Calanus
glacialis and Calanus hyperboreus have been widely studied regarding
their response to exposure to oil and its compounds (e.g., polycyclic
aromatic hydrocarbons (PAH)), as well as related chemical substances
(e.g., surface washing agents and dispersants). [Some findings suggest a
significant risk of oil component accumulation in Arctic copepods due to
their high lipid content (exceeding 50 % of dry weight) and their
limited ability to metabolize them]{.underline} (Øverjordet et al.,
2018). Acute toxicity data, such as the lethal concentration 50 (LC50),
were obtained from temperate, boreal, and Arctic species through
toxicity studies (Jager et al., 2017), indicating that Arctic species
are less sensitive when exposed to water-accommodated fractions (WAF) of
oil, probably due to lipid content and low temperature. [Sublethal
effects of oil were also studied through the investigation of
physiological indicators of these species, including fecal pellet
production, egg production, hatching rate and success, naupliar
development and length, and deformity (Hjorth and Nielsen, 2011; Jensen
and Carroll, 2010). Spilled oil has varying degrees of negative effects
on physiological performance, which may affect the overall health,
population dynamics, and survival and reproduction rates of exposed
species.]{.underline} In situ burning can be conducted to reduce the
potential effects of spilled oil. This was found to significantly
decrease the PAH exposure to Calanus hyperboreus, thus mitigating the
possible side effects on such species (Fritt-Rasmussen et al., 2024).

Research on the effects of spilled oil on Arctic and Subarctic
invertebrates is limited and has mainly focused on crustaceans and
molluscs. [The Arctic scallop Chlamys islandica widely inhabits Arctic
and Subarctic waters and shows promise as a sentinel organism for
environmental monitoring]{.underline} (Nahrgang et al., 2013). [Acute
oil exposure]{.underline} (e.g., WAF of North Sea crude oil with maximum
ΣPAH 163 μg/L for up to 21 days, dispersed Ekofisk crude oil at 0.06 and
0.25 mg/L for up to 15 days, and marine diesel with 29 ppm for 7 days)
[can affect scallop\'s immune function and oxidative stress status,
resulting in mortalities, phagocytosis and cell membrane stability
impairment, glutathione decrease, and other biological
effects]{.underline} (Hannam et al., 2010; Milinkovitch et al., 2015).
Similarly, [oil, chemically dispersed oil, and dispersant solution
treatments]{.underline} (five treatments of Alaska North Slope crude oil
and Corexit 9500 for up to 21 days: 10 ppm oil, 1 ppm dispersant, and 10
ppm oil with 2, 1, or 0.5 ppm dispersant) [exhibited acute and chronic
effects on the mussel Mytilus trossulus, showing genetic damage, tissue
loss, and a continued stress response; non-dispersed oil also suppressed
immune activity and caused higher mortality of exposed
mussels]{.underline} (Counihan, 2018). The effects of spilled oil on
invertebrates depend on exposure conditions, such as oil type,
concentration, and exposure duration. Consequently, the relevant static
laboratory results do not necessarily reflect the effects in the field
due to significantly different exposure regimes. However, mesocosm
studies and field observations, though limited, can be combined with
laboratory experiments to improve understanding of the ecological risks
of oil and to develop more effective management strategies in cold
regions.

2.2. Fish

[Polar cod]{.underline} (Boreogadus saida) [is highly abundant in the
Arctic Ocean and adjacent seas, and its geographical distribution may
overlap regions for petroleum activities with a high likelihood of oil
exposure. It serves as a monitoring species for the evaluation of the
impacts of spilled oil, and most examined biomarkers (e.g., enzyme
activity, metabolites, and gene and DNA damage) exhibit significant
responses to both waterborne exposure and dietary exposure associated
with petroleum-related substances]{.underline} (Nahrgang et al., 2010;
Vieweg et al., 2017).

[Due to the buoyancy of polar cod eggs, enhanced oil exposure to the
chorion]{.underline} (an acellular envelope surrounding the oocyte) [of
fish embryos, induces a decline in hatching success, disruption of heart
function, and abnormalities within larvae]{.underline} (Laurel et al.,
2019; Nahrgang et al., 2016). In early life stages, [normal polar cod
larvae development was considerably reduced, and the frequency and
severity of developmental abnormalities increased with increasing crude
oil]{.underline} (Kobbe crude) [exposure]{.underline} levels from 0.5 to
6 g oil per kg gravel (Nahrgang et al., 2016). Gardiner et al. (2013)
also studied the acute toxicity of 3 WAF of Alaska North Slope crude oil
with/without breaking wave and dispersant on the juvenile polar cod and
larval sculpin (Myoxocephalus sp.). They determined their median lethal
concentration ranges (1.6--4.0 mg/L for WAF and breaking wave WAF
treatments and 22--62 mg/L for chemically enhanced WAF treatment). The
synergistic adverse effects of oil exposure and warming on the embryos
and larvae of polar and Atlantic cod have also been reported by Bender
et al. (2021).

For adult polar cod, the growth rate was temporarily depressed when
treated with mechanically and chemically (dispersant FINASOL®) dispersed
Goliat (Kobbe) crude oil at 5 % w/w for 48 h; however, the overall
growth and survival were not affected in the long-term (Bender et al.,
2018). The mitochondrial metabolism of adult polar cod was also
investigated, and the results showed that crude Arabian light at 95 mg/L
for up to 48 h inhibited the activity of the mitochondrial respiratory
chain (Dussauze et al., 2014). In the sensitive life stage (e.g.
spawning period) of adults, polar cod females exposed to Barents sea
Goliat crude oil at 24 g oil per kg gravel were more inclined to spawn,
exhibited altered hepatic gene expression, and yielded eggs with
increased PAH body burden than their counterparts in the control group
(Strople et al., 2023). Exposure to in-situ burn oil residues
(approximately 4 L burned residue of fresh Kobbe crude oil in 200 L of
seawater for 48 h) induced fewer females to spawn but had no long-term
effects on male reproductive development (Bender et al., 2018). It can
be seen that the effects of oil exposure on Arctic fish can be
influenced by oil type and concentration, level of dispersion, exposure
route, life stage, sex, and the influence of response strategies (e.g.,
application of oil dispersants and in-situ burning).

2.3. Birds

[Seabirds are among the most vulnerable animal groups during oil spill
events, mostly because oil spills on the sea surface readily adhere to
their feathers, thereby changing their hydrophobic properties. This
results in a loss of insulation, waterproofing, and buoyancy, leading to
subsequent hypothermia, starvation, or drowning to death, which are
exacerbated in cold Arctic and Subarctic areas]{.underline}
(Fritt-Rasmussen et al., 2016). Meanwhile, oil residues from in situ
burning also showed comparable or potentially more harmful impacts on
seabird feathers, whose microstructure damage was detected even in cases
of thin, imperceptible sheens (Fritt-Rasmussen et al., 2016). [Some
seabirds, such as Pigeon Guillemots, also use nearshore habitats for
breeding and roosting on shorelines where oil contamination is likely to
occur]{.underline} (Helm et al., 2014a). [When the spilled oil reaches
the coastal areas, shorebirds are threatened by oil adhesion to their
feathers, ingestion by preening oil from contaminated feathers,
consumption of oiled prey, and disturbance from shoreline cleanup
operations]{.underline} (Ma et al., 2022).

Following the 1989 Exxon Valdez oil spill, an extensive research program
was initiated to document the effects of oil spills on birds in Arctic
and Subarctic regions. Piatt et al. (1990) estimated the mortality of
100,000--300,000 birds using aerial and ship-based surveys and
extrapolated data on the number of deceased birds collected. In addition
to immediate impact, the analysis of bird abundance and distribution
implied the effects of oil were most apparent in the year of spill
occurred with Pigeon Guillemots particularly affected, while recovery
indications were observed for all bird species initially impacted by
oiling after two years (Murphy et al., 1997). However, the adverse
effects continued for some bird species such as harlequin duck, Pigeon
Guillemot, and murres even nine years after the spill (Irons et al.,
2000), whereas they persisted for less than 2.5 years for most of the
affected bird species (Day et al., 1997). Ten years after the incident,
[the continuous exposure to oil residues, through wider dietary breadth
such as fishes and invertebrates and inhabiting nearshore with oil
pollution, can cause the possible liver damage in adult Pigeon
Guillemots]{.underline} (increased aspartate aminotransferase and
lactate dehydrogenase activities were observed in the oiled region
relative to unoiled region) [and reduce survival rate to restrict the
population recovery]{.underline} (Golet et al., 2002); similar findings
on other indicators (e.g., blood parameters) and species (e.g.,
harlequin ducks and Barrow\'s goldeneyes) have also been reported
(Seiser et al., 2000; Trust et al., 2000). After 11--14 years, the
cumulative winter survival rates of hatch-year female harlequin ducks
were 0.766 and 0.758 for the control and oiled regions, respectively,
indicating that the direct impacts of oil pollution on the demography of
this species were substantially diminished (Esler and Iverson, 2010). In
addition to occasional large spills, chronic oil pollution\'s effects on
marine birds in cold regions, such as illegal dumping and oil seeps,
have also been studied (Text S1).

2.4. Mammals

[Iconic marine mammals observed within Arctic and Subarctic coastal
areas include whales, seals, and polar bears. Spilled oil can affect
these mammals through direct exposure routes, including ingestion,
inhalation, and dermal contact]{.underline} (O\'Hara and Becker, 2002).
Furthermore, [indirect effects on these mammals may be significant due
to the reduction in prey, habitat alteration, behavioral pattern
changes, and reproduction impairment]{.underline} (Helm et al., 2014b).
Cetaceans (e.g., whales) frequently observed in shallow coastal waters
have respiratory intervals from minutes to over 1 h; [inhaling oil
volatile components at the air/water interface is the primary immediate
threat in oil spill incidents, which might cause lung issues, liver
damage, neurological disorders, and even death]{.underline} (Helm et
al., 2014b). The killer whale Orcinus orca population was evaluated
using photo identification before and after the 1989 Exxon Valdez oil
spill. The resident and transient populations experienced losses of 33
and 41 %, respectively, and neither of them returned to their pre-spill
population levels even 16 years after the disaster (Matkin et al.,
2008).

[As pinnipeds, seals are extensively distributed along Arctic marine
coastlines and are easily exposed to spilled oil on land and water. They
mainly rely on blubber rather than fur for insulation]{.underline}
(Liwanag et al., 2012); [therefore, oil adhesion on seals may not
significantly impair regulating body temperature but might result in
their inability to swim and cause drowning]{.underline} (Aubin, 1988).
Stimmelmayr et al. (2018) investigated and analyzed the pathological
indicators on three oiled ringed and spotted seals (Phoca hispida and
Phoca largha) and found increased PAH concentrations in oiled seal
tissues compared with those in unoiled counterparts. Nevertheless, the
corresponding PAH levels were relatively low (below 50 ng/g in wet
weight irrespective of oiling status), which might be attributed to the
rapid metabolism and elimination of PAHs previously observed in
vertebrates. [Cardiac lesions, liver issues, gastric ulcers/erosions
were also discovered in oiled seals. In earlier years, six ringed seals
exposed to crude oil for 24 h in the field experiment were observed with
eye and kidney issues and potential liver lesions. In contrast, another
three seals in captivity all died within 71 min after oil introduction
in the same manner as in field, which could be attributed to the
combined stress of oiling and captivity]{.underline} (Geraci and Smith,
1976). Unlike seals, [polar bears]{.underline} (Ursus maritimus)
[primarily depend on pelage to maintain their temperature; therefore,
they are susceptible to hypothermia because the insulative value of fur
is considerably reduced once oiled during spill incidents]{.underline}
(Hurst and Øritsland, 1982). [They also can ingest oil during grooming
or consuming oiled prey (e.g. seals) and inhale oil vapor to cause organ
troubles and harm respiratory and nervous systems]{.underline} (Jordan,
2013). Wilson et al. (2018) simulated a worst-case scenario for an oil
spill and estimated that up to 40 % of polar bears from the Chukchi Sea
could be affected. [Both seals and polar bears are highly dependent on
sea ice for essential activities such as hunting and resting. The
contamination of sea ice by spilled oil not only affects these
ice-dependent species but also causes cascading ecological effects
through the food web.]{.underline} Overall, our current knowledge and
understanding of the impacts of oil on Arctic marine mammals remains
limited due to small sample sizes and challenging field conditions.

2.5. Human

Arctic oil spills significantly threaten the health of coastal residents
and other people involved in cleanup operations. First, [oil spills can
cause physical/physiological effects on humans, including eye and dermal
irritation, respiratory issues, and headaches]{.underline} (Peres et
al., 2016); Second, [it can lead to mental health problems in people
exposed to oil. For example, people affected after the 1989 Exxon Valdez
oil spill exhibited a higher prevalence rate of generalized anxiety
disorder and posttraumatic stress disorder]{.underline}, with females
showing particular vulnerability (Palinkas et al., 1993). Indigenous
communities and fishermen experience more pronounced negative
psychosocial effects than other demographic groups (Picou and Gill,
1997). Third, [the harmful effects of oil on humans also can manifest
through genotoxicity, immunotoxicity, and endocrine
toxicity]{.underline} (Laffon et al., 2013; Laffon et al., 2016).
Pérez-Cadahía et al. (2008) revealed that [people with cleanup tasks
exposed for longer durations exhibited higher micronucleus frequency and
lower proliferation indices, generating chronic human health
effects.]{.underline}

Large oil spill incidents and related cleanup operations have also
generated adverse social impacts. [Spilled oil did not directly affect
the Cordova community in Alaska (a fishing-based economy) during the
1989 Exxon Valdez oil spill; however, the areas used for commercial
fishing and subsistence activities were contaminated and fish hatcheries
were also threatened, resulting in fish-related people and business
suffering huge losses]{.underline} (Russell et al., 2001). The Valdez
community in Alaska became the spill response center with a large influx
of oil spill cleanup personnel, placing substantial demands on community
and municipal services, such as elevated water consumption and airport
traffic (Rodin et al., 1992). Simultaneously, [public safety and health
resources become stretched with increased ambulance services, domestic
violence, crime rates, and social conflict]{.underline} (Gill et al.,
2012). For Alaska Natives, [the oil spill disaster not only seriously
affected their psychosocial well-being and disrupted their subsistence
activities but also disturbed their sociocultural milieu and core
cultural identity]{.underline} (Gill and Picou, 1997). [Many polluted
areas previously used for traditional hunting and fishing were closed,
significantly decreasing subsistence harvests; the safety issues of
these subsistence foods were also a major concern, forcing Alaska
Natives to rely on commercial food from outside the region]{.underline}
(Gill and Picou, 1997; Miraglia, 2002). [Oil pollution considerably
threatens the spiritual beliefs and daily behaviors of Native
communities.]{.underline}

[Oil spills can generate severe and persistent impacts on Arctic and
Subarctic wildlife and humans, and also can trigger cascading effects
throughout the ecosystem, affecting essential ecological processes and
resulting in long-term consequences.]{.underline} For example, [reducing
copepod populations due to oil pollution can directly decrease food
availability for predators, such as fish, birds, and marine mammals.
This may lead to population reductions of these predators and ultimately
disrupt ecological balance and ecosystem resilience.]{.underline}
Targeted regulations should be developed to protect these vulnerable
species, based on scientific evidence regarding the impacts of spilled
oil. Possible solutions include stricter controls on shipping routes and
oil exploration activities in sensitive areas, as well as long-term
monitoring and research programs after oil spills. Meanwhile, [oil
spills negatively affect the ecosystem services of Arctic and Subarctic
coastal areas, including fishing and tourism, cultural/recreational
values, climate regulation, and water quality.]{.underline} It is
necessary to assess the changes in ecosystem services to understand
comprehensive impacts of oil spills in cold regions. Including valuable
insights from indigenous communities ensures more culturally sensitive
and effective assessments and decision-making processes.

#### These natural resources are key to Native Alaskan survival.

Drew **McConville and** Alia **Hidayat**, 6-21-20**24** \[Drew
McConville is a senior fellow at Center for American Progress. He worked

Wildlife habitat and climate change

These public lands encompass about 6,700 miles of rivers and streams
accessible to fish migrating from the ocean---including five varieties
of Pacific salmon, among other species supported by these waters.11
Nearly 15 million acres of the lands under review also fall within the
range of the Western Arctic caribou herd,12 one of the largest caribou
herds on the planet.13 Abundant moose populations and a vast number of
migratory birds and other species also utilize these diverse habitats.14

Particularly as Alaska is warming two to three times faster than the
global average,15 [these intact and well-connected BLM public lands
represent a vital buffer against the impacts of climate change. Several
Alaskan wildlife populations have seen major declines in recent years,
and remaining populations rely heavily on intact, protected
lands.]{.underline}16 [Resilient populations of chinook and sockeye
salmon in the Bristol Bay watershed depend on the interconnected
"habitat mosaics" that characterize their spawning and rearing grounds,
utilizing different parts of the watershed at different times and
allowing them to adapt to shifting conditions.]{.underline}17 Likewise,
[three of North America's largest caribou herds depend on the BLM lands
under review, which support intact migration corridors necessary to
support robust populations.]{.underline}18 Revoking protections for
these lands and waters and allowing industrial development would
fragment habitat needed to protect vulnerable fish and wildlife
populations from the effects of climate change.

In addition to their climate resilience values, [these lands also hold
important reservoirs of natural carbon important for managing the
climate crisis.]{.underline} [The public lands under review have been
estimated to store]{.underline} 164 million tons of "irrecoverable
carbon," which is carbon that would not recover by midcentury if
released now by human activity.19 That equates to more than [600 million
tons of carbon dioxide]{.underline}, or the emissions from burning
roughly 8 million tanker trucks' worth of gasoline.20

Alaska Native subsistence resources

[Approximately 80 percent of Alaska Native villages (183 total) and more
than half a million people live within 50 miles of the BLM lands under
evaluation.]{.underline}21 [Of the lands under review, 74 percent are
designated by the federal government as areas where rural community
residents have priority to hunt and fish for subsistence.]{.underline}22

[Subsistence harvesting is a foundational element of Alaska Native
traditions, as well as an important source of food, economic stability,
and other needs.]{.underline}23 [The overwhelming majority of rural
Alaskans rely on food harvested from the surrounding lands and waters,
with 86 percent of rural households consuming wild game and 95 percent
consuming wild fish.24 More than a method of obtaining nutrition,
subsistence is a pillar of Alaska Native culture and a process of
communal sharing and generational bonding.]{.underline}25

Under the Alaska National Interest Lands Conservation Act, federal
agencies are required to consider and minimize adverse impacts to
subsistence uses in public land use decisions.26 In passing the law in
1980, Congress found that ["the continuation of the opportunity for
subsistence uses ... is essential to Native physical, economic,
traditional, and cultural existence."]{.underline}27

Revoking D-1 protections would open those [public lands to industrial
minerals development]{.underline}, resulting in likely [impacts to fish
and wildlife populations and displacement of subsistence
activities.]{.underline}28 Additionally, lifting D-1 protections and
subsequent conveyance of some of those lands to the state of Alaska
would result in Alaska Native communities and other rural residents
losing priority for subsistence use of fish and wildlife resources
offered by the federal government on federal lands.29 This subsistence
priority enables subsistence activities locally, with beneficial harvest
limits and hunting seasons and according to traditional customs.30

#### They're also key to cultural heritage. 

Alexandra **Fay**, 7-13-20**23** \[Alexandra Fay is an Assistant

A. An Inuit Framework for Food Security and Food Sovereignty

In 2020, the ICC Alaska published Food Sovereignty and Self-Governance:
Inuit Role in Managing Arctic Marine Resources. The 144-page report
documents resource management in Inuit communities in Alaska and the
Inuvialuit Settlement Region of Canada. [It analyzes the legislative and
regulatory structures that govern Inuit food security and evaluates
existing co-management schemes. Moreover, the report offers a framework
for understanding food security rooted in Indigenous Knowledge, using
theoretical tools devised by and for Inuit. The ICC Alaska framework
recognizes that [food security]{.mark} involves **[more than material
subsistence]{.mark}** because [food-based practices]{.mark} are [bound
up with]{.mark} **[spirituality, linguistics, knowledge, environmental
health, and self-governance]{.mark}**.]{.underline}33 The report
ultimately asserts that food security requires ["food sovereignty,"
[defined as:]{.mark}]{.underline}

[the [right]{.mark} of all Inuit [to define]{.mark} their [own hunting,
gathering, fishing, land, and water policies;]{.mark} the [right to
define]{.mark} [what is]{.mark} [sustainably, socially, economically,
and culturally appropriate for the distribution of food and to maintain
ecological health;]{.mark} and the [right to obtain and maintain
practices that ensure]{.mark} access to tools needed to [obtain,
process, store, and consume traditional foods.]{.mark}]{.underline}34

While ICC Alaska uses the language of rights to describe the concept of
food sovereignty, structurally, [the distinction between food security
and food sovereignty involves power. [Food sovereignty ensures food
security]{.mark} insofar as it [requires Native control of Native
foods,]{.mark} which [in turn requires control over resources and
land.]{.mark}]{.underline}35

[This notion of food sovereignty stands apart from Alaska's existing
resource management strategies, including models developed by
progressive management researchers and regulators who embrace the
rhetoric of co-management and Indigenous Knowledge.]{.underline} As Part
III details, [academic and professional discussions of subsistence
rights and resource management feature a limited space for change,
restricted by technocratic and colonial assumptions. These restrictive
visions of co-management seek to fit Indigenous consultants and
Indigenous Knowledge into the existing American management
framework.]{.underline} They fail to imagine a real transfer of
decision-making power to Natives, let alone management regimes
structurally informed by Native worldviews. To achieve the ICC Alaska
notion of food sovereignty, the discourse of co-management must be
upset.

#### Mining is key to Indigenous energy sovereignty and powers local economies. 

Daniel **Raimi &** Alana **Davicino 24**, Fellow at Resources for the
Future, Lecturer at the University of Michigan Gerald R. Ford School of
Public Policy, M.P.P. from the Duke University Sanford School of Public
Policy; Budget Analyst at the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency,
M.P.A. from the Indiana University O\'Neill School of Public and
Environmental Affairs, "Securing energy sovereignty: A review of key
barriers and opportunities for energy-producing Native nations in the
United States," Energy Research & Social Science, Vol. 107, January
2024, accessed 07-21-2025, https://doi.org/10.1016/j.erss.2023.103324,
DG

Understanding issues of tribal energy sovereignty in the United States
requires researchers to look back to the first interactions between
Native Americans and European settlers. Since the establishment of the
United States, [federal government actions towards Native nations have
evolved from genocidal to discriminatory to]{.underline}, in more recent
years, [halting efforts to secure the **sovereignty** and **resources**
to which Native nations are entitled. When tribes possess energy
sovereignty, they have **agency in determining whether development will
occur**, and]{.underline} if it does, [how that]{.underline} development
[will affect their lands and people]{.underline}.

Although Native nations and their citizens will determine the direction
of their own economies, the federal government can support tribal energy
sovereignty by providing financial and technical assistance to support
the development of those plans, then make them a reality. Despite the
fact that federal agencies have established programs and tools dedicated
to energy development on reservation land, their effectiveness to date
appears quite limited, and has not been carefully examined in the
literature. Such evaluation could be a useful tool to improve upon
existing programs and further support Native nations in their
energy-related decision making.

[As the US and the world transition towards]{.underline} a
[net-zero]{.underline} emissions future, [tribes whose economic
well-being are closely tied to fossil fuel development **may need to
diversify their economies or use revenue from fossil fuels as a
springboard for other investments**. Some Native nations]{.underline},
such as the Crow, Navajo, and Northern Cheyenne have [already
experienced **substantial economic disruptions** associated with the
closure of coal mines and power plants]{.underline} (e.g., \[110,111\]).
[Others]{.underline}, such as the Southern Ute Indian Tribe, [have built
large financial assets that can provide an economic cushion against
a]{.underline} potential [decline in oil and]{.underline} natural [gas
production]{.underline}.

In addition, some Native nations are pursuing innovative technologies
that utilize their fossil fuel resources in lower- and zero-emitting
technologies. For example, the MHA Nation is pursuing a partnership to
provide natural gas to a nearby hydrogen manufacturing facility \[112\].
As another example, the SUIT are pursuing a partnership to deploy an
innovative natural gas-fired power generation technology that produces a
pure stream of CO2 that can readily be sold (e.g., for enhanced oil
recovery) or stored \[113\]. In addition, the declining costs of
renewable energy technologies coupled with changes in federal policy may
speed the deployment of clean energy on reservation land \[63,90,114\].

Whatever Native nations may decide, they will need access not only to
standard analytical tools such as environmental impact assessments and
benefit-cost analyses, but also tools that account for the cultural,
spiritual, and other resources specific to each tribe. The development
and deployment of such decision aids has been limited in the past, and
their expansion could further support tribal energy sovereignty.

5\. Conclusion

For centuries, [Native nations in the **U**nited **S**tates have worked
to ensure]{.underline} the [full exercise of their sovereign
rights]{.underline}. In this review, we examine the key barriers to, and
tools to secure, tribal sovereignty over their decisions related to
energy production. Although there is a long history of injustice
regarding Native nations\' interactions with the US federal government,
[tribes have taken a variety of approaches to enhance their control over
their energy futures]{.underline}. In recent decades, [Native nations
have adopted innovative governance approaches and wielded political
power in support of this]{.underline} goal. More recently, new federal
policies, new technologies, and innovative indigenous-led approaches
have opened up possibilities for new energy development on tribal lands,
including fossil- and non-fossil fuel-based technologies. Looking
forward, substantial barriers remain to securing energy sovereignty for
Native nations. However, these recent developments offer cause for
cautious optimism.

#### Co-management and co-design helps mitigate the harms oil spills.

Macey A. **McCann** 6-30-20**21** \[Macey A. McCann, Master of

https://doi.org/10.17863/CAM.84043, accessed 7-14-2025 // ava (barbiere)

\-\--NC = Native Cooperation

\-\--FPIC = Free, Prior and Informed Consent
