**[Chapter 4: Value Proposition for Companies, Benefit Sharing for
Communities]{.underline}**

External Drivers

[In 2012, Lloyd's of London predicted that the Arctic "will attract
\$100 billion in new investment over the next decade" but "urged
companies not to 'rush in'" (Ward cited in Kroh and Conathan, 2012).
They noted \"hard-to-manage risks\" including knowledge gaps,
challenging and unpredictable conditions, potentially worse
environmental consequences, and \"politics of Arctic economic
development are controversial and fluid\" (Kroh and Conathan,
2012).]{.underline} In conjunction with the operational risks noted by
Lloyds, companies operating in the Arctic must consider reputational
risks, legal risks, and the costs of company-community conflict. [These
risks create external drivers that are the premise for policies like the
IFC Performance Standards and motivate companies to implement risk
reduction policies such as **benefit sharing**. Reputational risks arise
from failure to gain a **social license to operate** (SLO) and external
perceptions of a company\'s compliance with international standards,
such as FPIC (See Table 1). Not taking SLO seriously, companies may
\"forfeit millions of dollars due to operational delays\...due to
**resistance of indigenous peoples**\" and social rights activists (Lehr
and Smith, 2010).25]{.underline} Legal risks include injunctions by
courts halting exploration, e.g. Alaska Wilderness League v. Kempthorne,
and operation and suits filed against companies that require lengthy
litigation. Company-community conflict can result in considerable costs,
including elevated security, project modification, material damage, lost
productivity, loss of future market access, and more (Davis and Franks,
2014). Since multinational oil and gas companies self-fund these
projects, they are less responsive to standards set by international
lenders and primarily concerned with reputation, insurance, and
litigation.

This dissertation aims to help extractive companies recognize [\"there
is a positive proposition behind it, particularly in places like the
Arctic because there\'s a lot of things that can be learned \[from\]
traditional communities\" (Interview Slaiby, 2021).]{.underline} An
often overlooked layer is the timeframe of different industries to
quantify and respond to these ESG pressures. [In the resource extraction
industry, where operations can last several decades, few are aware and
fully understand the long process that pre-empts any exploration and
development.]{.underline} Slaiby (Interview 2021) shared with me how
\"IOCs (international oil companies) in general are thinking of
decades-long relationships\". He explains that when \"\[communities\]
wanted the best of both worlds, \[Shell\] said fine because not doing
that would create conflict \[and\] when times get tough\...they\'re
going to be jumping right down your throat\" (Interview 2021). He
contrasts this to \"private equity \[and\] smaller cap companies\" that
have \"a smaller investment window, there\'s different pressures, and
there\'s lack of pressures on some things\". This differentiation in
project life cycles is a motivating factor for companies and a
potentially novel consideration for activists and academics in
sustainability.

[As shown by the Alaskan Model, **benefit sharing agreements** that
empower indigenous communities to utilize **TK** and take ownership of
projects **mitigates** a number of these risks.]{.underline}26 West
(Interview 2021) explains that in \"extractive industries\...no matter
what agreement is reached with the government; no matter what benefit
sharing has worked out with Indigenous people; no matter what
environmental practices they evoke\", contracts will continually be
renegotiated depending on demand and price of mineral commodities. From
his years of experience working in the Overseas Private Investment
Corporation (OPIC), he understands \"it\'s a political reality
\[extractive companies\] have to deal with\" (Interview 2021).
[Nonetheless, \"spending a little more time getting it right from the
get-go\" will build a foundation for open and respectful renegotiation
rather than litigation (Interview Slaiby, 2021). These external drivers
to reduce ESG risks are only half of the argument for respectfully
engaging and partnering with these communities.]{.underline} The case
studies below demonstrate how benefit sharing and working with, not
against or around, indigenous communities can create benefits that flow
both ways.

Internal Drivers

[A surprising finding of my conversations with extractive industry
managers and indigenous leaders, which in Alaska often overlap, was
their appreciation for the same values and the long term perspective.27
The alliances and relationships formed between these historically
opposed groups were primarily fostered by shared interests rather than
external drivers, not excluding their shared desire for
income.]{.underline} When I asked [Slaiby]{.underline}, \"what were the
motivating factors that made you want to partner with these
communities?\" the first thing he said was \"respect for elders\"
(Interview 2021). He [genuinely valued and was motivated by the ethos of
Alaska Natives. He highlighted their \"views on family and lack of
materialism\" and especially \"their love of food and their
reverence\...the idea that you take only what you need\" lastly
\"sharing, absolutely important\" (Interview 2021). Similarly, when
Hensley (Interview 2021) spoke of Inuit values, he said, \"we treated
each other with respect, we respected our elders, we loved our children,
we worked hard, we had a sense of humour, we understood our environment,
we share, \[and\] we cooperated\". These two did not verbally
communicate about these values. Rather they were observed and known
through the experience of co-inhabiting Alaska, e.g. hunting, camping,
and dining together]{.underline}. The policies and approaches (see
Table 1) nor western corporate etiquette promote this \'way of knowing\'
and sharing cultures and benefits.

[The unforced and unspoken method of sharing values reveals that
incorporating indigenous ethos into corporate consciousness is possible
but requires empathy, sociability, and keeping an open mind. Slaiby
drives home the importance of \"the right mindset, and\...the right
values\" for outside companies. He explains, "what you're doing is
you're setting up a situation that's going to be way **more
sustainable**\...you're building on concrete, rather than sand"
(Interview 2021). That way, \"when things go wrong, which inevitably,
they do...\[they\] had built the foundation on bedrock in Alaska by
being \[themselves\] and really deeply caring about the organization
\[they\] put together\" (Interview Slaiby, 2021).]{.underline} Glenn
(Interview 2021) suggested another way to share indigenous expertise is
\"if you develop an alliance, like \'I trust you. You trust me\'\" then
indigenous people will \"think it\'s important to\...share with you\".
Building trust is so critical that \"if \[outside workers\] had no touch
with the communities \[Slaiby\] stopped them from going into the
communities\" (Interview 2021). He explained that \"\[Shell\] hired
people who legitimately cared about the cultures that they work in\"
(Interview Slaiby, 2021). It was easier to build relationships \"because
\[Alaska Natives and non-native workers\] were kind of cut from the same
cloth\...the guys who work in the extraction areas\...grew up hunting,
fishing and outdoors and\...there was kind of an affinity\" (Interview
Slaiby, 2021). [Beyond the external drivers, only empathetic corporate
leadership will drive change in corporate consciousness.]{.underline} My
research leads me to believe that external drivers alone cannot create
the genuine trust needed to facilitate sustainable partnerships for
benefit sharing.

Looking through the governance lens of the ESG kaleidoscope, [corporate
leadership will need to have business and emotional intelligence to
create resilient and sustainable development. Because indigenous
education develops a multidimensional perspective, indigenous people can
lead with the head and the heart.]{.underline} For example, Edward Itta
was \"a whaling captain\" responsible for the lives of his crew and as
Mayor of the North Slope Borough, he \"negotiated \[monetary benefits\]
with oil companies\" (Waldholz, 2016). Reiss says, \"whether he was
talking with an Inupiaq person\...or an oil person, \[Itta\] could
really feel your heart, and respond to it, as one human does to
another\" (Waldholz, 2016). [Executives of extractive companies should
feel internally motivated to work with NCs that already embody this
resilient, pragmatic governance model.]{.underline} Saliby's perspective
and close work with Itta exemplify the empathetic leadership for which
this dissertation argues.

Despite popular opinion, he is not an anomaly. [Mack (Interview, 2021)
said, "my impression of oil companies is\...most of the people\...view
themselves as a big part of making the world a better place; \[they\]
want to view their work as being something greater than just making a
buck".]{.underline} That corporate consciousness parallels the
previously stated concerns of Aaron Schutt, Willie Hensley, Richard
Glenn, and Edward Itta. [Once again, Slaiby\'s internal motivations
align with his indigenous counterparts, highlighting why he built trust
where others have failed. An excellent practice note for empathetic
leadership was setting personal goals for **success**.]{.underline}
Slaiby (Interview, 2021) explains how he decided that he was \"going to
be successful and enjoy \[working in Alaska\], if \[he\'s\]
left\...things a little better\". Slaiby\'s retrospection demonstrates
the self-reflection for corporate leaders considering whether or not
they have the right mentality to partner with indigenous communities in
the Arctic.

Case Studies of Benefit Sharing

[The following cases provide good practice examples for benefit sharing
agreements between outside companies and indigenous communities in the
Alaskan Arctic]{.underline}. These Alaskan cases highlight the
initiative and foresight shown by indigenous leaders and outside company
leadership even before many approaches to benefit sharing were fully
formed (See Table 1).

NANA- Teck Red Dog Mine

[The Red Dog mine ("Red Dog") is one of the world's largest zinc mines
located north of the Arctic Circle in northwest Alaska. Starting in
1982, Red Dog developed a system of "collaborative governance" using an
"innovative operating agreement between mining companies and indigenous
people" (Sustainable Mining through Collaborative Governance at Red Dog
Operations, no date).]{.underline} Hensley was the president of NANA
corporation at the time and negotiated the terms of the benefit sharing
partnership of Red Dog. He explains, \"we were able to get what we
wanted\...because we own and control\...this multi-billion dollar
resource that was the richest in the world\...at the time and so that
gave us a lot of clout at the negotiating table\" (Interview Hensley,
2021). [As owners of the land, the NC negotiates the same agreement as
non-native landowner leasing to developers. However, because the NANA
Regional Corporation represents Inupiat shareholders, the company must
consider the principles of benefit sharing and create value for a wider
community. Hensley lists the unique ways they altered the western
operating agreement to meet the needs of the communities in the
Northwest Alaskan Arctic. The agreement included \"strict indigenous
hire provisions\...reporting requirements\...and \[instituted\] a
subsistence committee that was responsible for environmental issues\"
(Interview Hensley, 2021).]{.underline} Teck empowered the Alaska
Natives through the Management Committee made of six representatives
from Teck and six from NANA (Sustainable Mining through Collaborative
Governance at Red Dog Operations, no date). [The committee has
"oversight of exploration, mineral development and production" and
ensures "safeguarding the physical, cultural, economic, social and
subsistence needs of the residents in the NANA region" (Sustainable
Mining through Collaborative Governance at Red Dog Operations, no
date).]{.underline} Hensley (Interview 2021) highlights "the substantial
power that was given to nearby villages through this subsistence
committee". Similar to the North Slope Borough, ["the Red Dog
mine\...provides the revenue... to have a **regional government**, which
is very beneficial to our communities" (Interview Hensley, 2021). The
NANA-Teck agreement demonstrates both the **local hire and ownership**
types of benefit sharing in Chapter 3.]{.underline}

[Similar to how Hensley described the ANCSA Corporations, NANA filled
the empty construct of an operating agreement by incorporating
indigenous values and TK. The horizontal power relationship between the
outside company as operator and indigenous communities as owner allowed
for this prime example of benefit sharing built on a solid foundation of
trust. In 2019, 57% of Red Dog\'s workforce were NANA shareholders, and
\$155 million was spent on indigenous suppliers (Sustainable Mining
through Collaborative Governance at Red Dog Operations, no
date).]{.underline} In addition to monetary benefits, Red Dog supports
programs like the John Baker Youth Leaders program, \"an anti-bullying
and suicide prevention program\" (Westlake, 2016). [Louis Steele speaks
about the benefits Red Dog provides to the villages. She
\"encourages\[s\] young women to come and work up at Red Dog\" because
\"if \[she\] could work in a man\'s world and succeed then they can do
it too\"(Steele, 2016).]{.underline} Her video was prominently featured
on Teck\'s Red Dog web page, implying that the partnership may have
influenced greater transparency and concern for the lives and voices of
stakeholders. That exemplifies the change in corporate consciousness
that this dissertation aims to promote.

NSB- Shell Offshore Oil and Gas

The case of Royal Dutch Shell\'s oil and gas exploration in Alaska from
2012 to 2015 has was frequently covered by outside news sources, most
commonly noting that the company "spent \$7 billion on an unsuccessful
drilling campaign" (Humpert, 2020 par.6). The following case study
presents an intimate account of the \'campaign\' through the
perspectives of the prominent leaders directly involved. [Unlike most
reports of Shell\'s activities, my research focuses on Shell\'s positive
progress working with communities. The first lesson for outsiders is
that "in Alaska\...you're going to get your ass handed to you\...you're
gonna get knocked down \[but\] it's how you get back up when people
judge you" (Interview Slaiby, 2021).]{.underline}

[After the Alaska Wilderness League v. Kempthorne injunction in 2007,
Shell\'s lease was approved in 2009. After that, however, they \"decided
\[they\] weren\'t going to drill...\[they\] wanted to be into these
communities in a different way\", so they utilized a program called 3D
negotiation (Interview Slaiby, 2021). 28 Slaiby explains how the Shell
team \"spent nine months...\[and\] mapped out every stakeholder
relationship\". The main point Slaiby (Interview 2021) made was \"**you
have to go slow to go fast**\...establishing those relationships for
years, going to the festivals, getting invited to the festivals, really
appreciating the culture\". What came out of \"a lot of soul searching\"
and years of negotiations put an **interesting twist on benefit
sharing** (Interview Slaiby, 2021).]{.underline} The most significant
change was Shell asked the ASRC to purchase overriding shares in a
jointventure, the Arctic Inupiat Offshore (AIO), \"to share the
development cost, bring in \[their\] views, \[their\] opinion\...as a
partner\". Treadwell (Interview 2021) described it as \"empowered
ownership\". [Shell incorporating the NC as equity partners redefined
the premises of benefit sharing because not only revenue but also costs
were divided.]{.underline} Although this seems counterintuitive to
benefit sharing, I want to reiterate Hensley\'s point that ownership is
the purest form of self-determination.

[When Shell started planning, Slaiby (Interview 2021) says, \"the
standards for drilling in the Arctic weren\'t all there\...we had to
work with \[ASRC\] on acceptable standards\".]{.underline} Creating the
NSB Shell Baseline Studies Program was where Itta and Slaiby aligned
their interests. Slaiby (Interview 2021) found \"\[Itta\] wanted that
economic stability, but \[without\] sacrificing \[the\] cultural
touchstone\...the subsistence lifestyle\". [Shell \"worked with them on
traditional knowledge\" giving indigenous experts a \"blank check\" and
\"didn\'t edit anything that they thought was important\" (Interview
Slaiby, 2021). He says that the western scientists were there to \"make
sure everything was done right...\[and\] frankly \[they\] never saw any
huge gaps\...maybe some of the whys were different, but the phenomena
were largely there\" (Interview 2021).]{.underline} 29Few outside of the
company and community recognize the trust and effort it took to complete
this ground breaking cross-cultural project that still functions today.

Now that Slaiby and Itta had a relationship, the next step was to
identify the community\'s needs. [Shell put their \"people from oil and
gas into the communities\...to explain what oil and gas might mean\" but
also \"be there to talk to people, find out what\'s underneath, what
their needs are and what their concerns are\" (Interview Slaiby, 2021).
When they listened, they ended up \"finding ways to bring
opportunity\...from subsistence advisers, marine mammal observers, embed
oil spill response people,\...and finding really good ways\...we could
put good people to work\" (Interview Slaiby, 2021). Another important
item that Slaiby focused on was not to displace people, i.e. the curse
of oil. So Shell \"brought in temporary camps\", ensured outside workers
\"weren\'t cleaning out grocery store shelves\", and \"focused on
creating the supply chain that actually benefits the community\"
(Interview Slaiby, 2021). All of this work integrating and ensuring the
sustainability of the community built a strong trust between the North
Slope people and Shell.]{.underline}

The relationship was tested on New Years of 2012 when a drillship going
from Dutch Harbor to Seattle broke loose and grounded on Kodiak Island.
Slaiby (Interview 2021) describes how \"they showed up in an instant,
knocking the ice off things\...we had the people behind us\". Shell
recovered quickly because \"we were very transparent about what we did\"
and \"for the four years\...we had really worked to try and be very
proficient in that space\" (Interview Slaiby, 2021). Reiss, the author
of The Eskimo and the Oil Man (2012), attested to Slaiby and Shell's
corporate consciousness of safety. [Reiss (Interview 2021) saw that
"when they made mistakes, their mistakes never came out of wanting to
abuse people\...the **mistakes were always** \[Shell's\] belief in their
own ability to do things **without consulting the indigenous
community**".]{.underline}

Slaiby worked closely with Edward Itta to \"make a lot of changes in
\[Shell\'s\] program that remain the rule of activity in the Arctic
today\" (Interview Mack, 2021). Slaiby exemplified empathetic leadership
and \"moved his company, he moved his own thinking, he did things, he
adopted \...a more indigenous view of the world\" (Interview Mack,
2021). [The biggest challenges were building trust after the injunction
in 2007, seeing from the other\'s perspective, and changing corporate
practices. Shell overcame these by creating value for all stakeholders
with 3D negotiation, co-producing science through the NSB Shell Baseline
Program, and using cultural awareness training led by indigenous to
foster ties between the Alaska Native and non-native
workers.]{.underline}

Kuupik Corporation- NSB - Oil Search

Since 1929, the multinational company Oil Search has built a positive
reputation for working with indigenous communities and operating in
challenging environments such as Papua New Guinea, Australia, and
Alaska. [Oil Search was suggested by Glenn (Interview 2021) as a
\"current good player\" because \"they took people on the land, and put
them to work at every strategy level within the company\...from
helicopter pilots and EMTs, to the lawyers and executives\". Oil Search
publicly promotes, \"**local employment \[is\] important for our social
license to operate**\" and accredits \"having a workforce that reflects
the areas where we operate strengthens our capability and
resilience\"(Sustainability: People, no date).]{.underline}

Their onshore operations in Alaska involve multiple native stakeholders,
including the North Slope Borough and Kuupik Corporation. [In the
\"pre-FEED\" (Front-End Engineering and Design) process, Oil Search
conducted a \"comprehensive independent Environmental Impact Study
(EIS)\...with **significant community engagement** and comment
periods\"(\'Material progress made on Pikka Unit Development, Alaska
North Slope, with major resource upgrade\', 2019). Mack explains how
Kuupik and Oil Search \"sat down at the table\...for six
months\...defining the geographic scope\...the subsistence terms, the
permit review\" before the company received the lease.]{.underline} The
first part of the conversation \"set up for \[Oil Search\] how \[they\]
view the world as an Inupiat group\" then \"the second part of the
conversation was the commercial aspects\" (Interview Mack, 2021).
[Consultation before leasing, Slaiby informed me, is uncommon since the
leases are through the federal government. However, **Oil Search\'s
approach aligns better with the principles of FPIC**. Responding to
concerns, Oil Search \"**slowed the process to incorporate more than 25
design improvements, to further minimize the environmental footprint and
local impact**\"(\'Material progress made on Pikka Unit Development,
Alaska North Slope, with major resource upgrade\', 2019). Oil Search\'s
practices exemplify and demonstrate that by **working closely with
communities through benefit sharing**, extractive companies benefit from
reduced ESG risks and indigenous environmental expertise.]{.underline}

Two important benefit sharing agreements that Oil Search incorporated
into their project were the re-zoning amendment requested by NSB and the
Land Use Agreement (LUA) with Kuupik Corporation. 30 [The zoning of the
development in the NSB is an essential part of benefit sharing because
revenue from property taxes paid by oil and gas development pay for many
social services (Interview Reiss, 2021). The amendment to the zoning map
of NSB \"modifies the land on which infrastructure\...will be
constructed from a conservation district to a resource development
district\"]{.underline} (\'Material progress made on Pikka Unit
Development, Alaska North Slope, with major resource upgrade\', 2019).

The LUA included \"Inupiat driven phrases, concepts, languages into a
western-style contract\...which allows them to be on our land, but they
have to...\[make\] sure that subsistence is active, thriving, and
protected\" (Interview Mack, 2021). The specific section that
incorporated these priorities was \"Guiding Principles and Intents\".
Mack (Interview 2021) paraphrased to me:

• [Oil search agrees to consult the Kuupik and give special
considerations to traditional knowledge regarding subsistence resources,
including migratory habitat pass, traditional hunting and trapping
areas.]{.underline}

• [Oil Search acknowledges that such subsistence considerations may
change over time. And it shall design its operations to minimize impacts
on the community of Nuiqsut cultural and subsistence practices and
protect and promote wildlife resources and subsistence.]{.underline}

• [Oil Search will incorporate requests relating to traditional
knowledge into its project design where practical.]{.underline}

The flexible language of this section reflects the \'conditions of
uncertainty\' mentioned by West and ensures that the main points of TK
and subsistence are enshrined. [Unlike consultation in FPIC, \"if they
don\'t do these things, this is a contract that \[Kuupik\] can take to
court\" (Interview Mack, 2021). The enforceability of this contract
distinguishes it from the standards of benefit sharing and consultation,
which operate more as guidance than a checklist.]{.underline} Applying
lessons from my research, the Oil Search's preliminary steps and
resulting agreements indicate a strong positive trajectory for their
development on the North Slope.

Practical Guidance

This dissertation has examined the challenges, considerations, and value
of benefit sharing agreements in the context of indigenous communities
in the Alaskan Arctic. The research shows that [outside companies,
especially the extraction industry, have the opportunity to create value
by partnering with local indigenous communities. However, acknowledging
the lack of enforceable and clear international governance, outside
companies should defer to local indigenous peoples for direction on
benefit sharing.]{.underline} From the analysis of lessons learned in
Alaska, the following protocol provides some practical guidance for
creating relationships, incorporating indigenous perspectives and
knowledge, and enhancing the sustainability of development.

1\. Recognizing the importance of empathetic leadership, [companies
should consider the project\'s leadership based on a genuine interest in
indigenous culture.]{.underline}

2\. To minimize reputational and legal risk, [companies should
incorporate to the extent possible international principles of free,
prior, and informed consent and self-determination into corporate
policies.]{.underline}

3\. Respecting the histories and sensitivities of indigenous
communities, [companies should provide cultural awareness training that
involves equal participation by outside workers and local
communities.]{.underline}

4\. Ensuring the minimal impact of development on ecology, subsistence
practices, and villages, [companies should take sufficient time to map
out stakeholder relationships and listen to stakeholder
concerns.]{.underline}

5\. Anticipating unforeseen difficulties of operating in the Arctic,
[companies should seek indigenous guidance and expertise early in
project planning, for example, understanding seasonal
ecosystems.]{.underline}

6\. Understanding the value of indigenous perspectives, [companies
should look for ways to incorporate indigenous people into all levels of
a project and empower their participation and leadership.]{.underline}

7\. Considering the life cycle of extraction projects, [companies should
co-produce policies on community engagement, value-laden principles and
intents, and establish a mechanism for conflict avoidance and
resolution.]{.underline}

The last three protocol specifically apply to consultation:

8\. [Enter a conversation full of empathy, \" with open \[mind\], open
ears, and an open heart\"; \"it\'s really more the spirit and intent of
coming forward...content driven, \[rather\] don\'t have an
agenda\"]{.underline} (Interview, Shorty 2021).

9\. [Seek out and \"ensure that the very best from the community is in
the room...that you have a fair representation...balance between men and
women...youth and elders\"]{.underline}

(Interview Shorty, 2021).

10\. Lastly, indigenous culture is very relational (Interview
Huntington, 2021). [Company representatives can show humility and
indicate openness by introducing their relations, to their family, to
the world around them, and lastly, to the project.]{.underline}

#### Native Alaskans want the plan, but only if they have an active role in economic development. It is key to maintaining their culture.

Alaska's oil and gas\", Financial Times,

The North Slope Iñupiat have stewarded their land that now includes the

to]{.underline} an average of **[77 years]{.underline}**. This is one of
the largest increases of its kind in the US and is [due]{.underline} in

President Trump's executive order to "unleash" energy production on the

**[Iñupiaq self-determination]{.mark}** in our homelands.]{.underline}

#### Only Native Alaskans co-management can solve\-\--they have an intricate knowledge of the environment that is absent otherwise. 

Macey A. **McCann** 6-30-20**21** \[Macey A. McCann, Master of
Philosophy from University of Cambridge, Senior Executive Editor at the
Baylor Law Review, 6-30-2021, "Empowering Indigenous Culture Through
Benefit Sharing Agreements: Study of Companies and Communities in the
Alaskan Arctic" Apollo - University of Cambridge Repository,
<https://doi.org/10.17863/CAM.84043>, accessed 7-14-2025 // ava
(barbiere) :)\]

Indigenous Education and Traditional Knowledge

When trying to understand and incorporate indigenous knowledge, it helps
to have an awareness of their epistemology. The first consideration
explained in this section is Indigenous education, i.e. ways of knowing,
presented through the stories and explanations of indigenous
participants. The second consideration, traditional knowledge, will be
presented through native and non-native perspectives. [They explain the
distinguishing features of land-based knowledge and the **value of
incorporating indigenous expertise**. My research leads me to believe
that western corporations need to think outside the box and look through
the broad view of indigenous education to sustainably co-develop the
Arctic. Kawagley explains that by \"trying to understand the parts to
understand the whole\" (categorization), \"\[western\] assumptions
(proposition) and expectations (syllogism) muddle **their** efforts to
see things as they really are\"(Kawagley, 1999 p.84). Norma Shorty
(Interview 2021) also describes the \"idea of a Western
methodology...\[as\] squares and blocks\...building blocks to knowledge
scaffolding\". On the other hand, they describe \"indigenous ways of
knowing\" as \"circular\" it-\"deals with the **whole physical,
intellectual, emotional, and spiritual of inner and outer
ecologies**\"(Kawagley, 1999 and Interview Shorty, 2021). This
circularity reflects both the connection of the knowledge to
environmental cycles but also human life cycles.]{.underline}

Creating a sustainable future requires both of these cycles to be in
harmony and indigenous people understand this better than outsiders
because they know the land. [Shorty (Interview 2021) notes it is
important \"to understand that indigenous knowledge is place based\" and
\"the stories and histories\...inform us how we can sustain our way of
life forever\".]{.underline} However, Glenn (Interview 2021) makes the
point that \"it\'s a use it or lose it culture\" and Schutt (Interview
2021) shared his concerns that too many youths were moving away from the
land. The ongoing migration from rural Alaska is detrimental to Arctic
knowledge since those growing up outside their lands lose the knowledge
from experience and the language skills to listen and learn from the
elders. To avoid further insertion of western epistemology, the
protection of indigenous culture \"belongs \[to\] the communities
\[but\] can be supported by these development players\" (Interview
Glenn, 2021).

Learning by observation, stories, and experience for thousands of years
has equip the indigenous communities of the Arctic with the knowledge to
innovate for, adapt to, and overcome challenges posed by their
environment. [Companies should consider how indigenous knowledge of the
land will affect their expectations for developers.]{.underline} Slaiby
(Interview 2021), former VP of Shell Alaska, explained that [\"what
traditional knowledge observes\...science cannot argue with the
phenomenon they\'ve been looking at for 12,000 years, they pretty much
got \[outsiders\] beat.\" Even if the indigenous experts cannot always
explain using scientific language, **they** understand their
environment. There are positive implications for western knowledge in
the Arctic because \"it can actually be very helpful in explaining some
of the phenomenon\" (Interview Slaiby, 2021). Arctic scientific
exploration has only scratched the tip of the iceberg. If outsiders \"do
it respectfully\...and use \[indigenous knowledge\] as a kind of mutual
expertise\...then \[they\] start to learn the lessons that have been
hard fought and paid for already in this traditional knowledge\"
(Interview Glenn, 2021).]{.underline} Much of this knowledge is embedded
in the language, making language conservation important for both
indigenous cultures and their partners. TK can assist with the areas of
working on and with sea ice and the protection of wildlife.

To demonstrate how [\"indigenous people wouldn\'t be consulted to the
detriment of both parties\"]{.underline}, Reiss (Interview 2021) told me
his experience in a Navy War Room simulation. When developing a response
plan to an oil spill in the Arctic, [there was no discussion about ice
until finally, Reiss himself mentioned, \"the ice comes in October\"; an
important consideration he picked up when working with Edward Itta
(Interview Reiss, 2021). Even in the 2010s, the **US Navy forgot the
existence of Alaska Natives as a necessary perspective for Arctic
planning**. Reiss (Interview 2021) explains that \"it never was
that...\[outsiders\] were looking down on locals, it was that they were
looking past locals\...they just didn\'t realize what they didn\'t know
\[and\] how they could maximize their own interests by cooperating with
locals\".]{.underline} There is a real danger for humans, for industry,
and for wildlife of missing important considerations, like understanding
sea ice, that are always on the minds of people who have lived in the
Arctic for millennia. Where outsiders see a \"beautiful painting of a
guy standing on an ice flow\", Edward Itta, former Mayor of the North
Slope Borough, sees \"a guy stranded on the ice flow because he didn\'t
pay attention\" (Itta cited in Interview Reiss, 2021).

[Including indigenous people in planning reduces the risk that outsiders
will miss an important consideration of TK. Even Slaiby, who \"drilled a
lot of wells\...in all kinds of areas\...harsh and benign environments\"
recognized that the \"\[DOI\] regulations aren\'t going to work \[in the
Arctic\]\"]{.underline} (Interview, 2021). Correspondingly, Edward Itta
said, \"the stuff you\'re doing won\'t work here\", so Slaiby worked
with Itta to \"recreate \[plans\] and \[put\] a program together that\'s
going to work for \[Alaska Natives and the Arctic\]\" (Interview 2021).
[Even when the federal government carries out **environmental impact
assessments** before leasing, their **outside scientists have
sometimes** fail**ed** to identify risks that indigenous people always
consider. Itta \"knew that his traditional knowledge was critically
important to his success in making sure the standards that apply for
offshore drilling were strong\" (Interview Mack, 2021).]{.underline} In
Alaska Wilderness League v. Kempthorne the 9th Circuit Court of Appeals
held that [the federal assessors "failed to take a 'hard look' at the
effects Shell\'s project would have on the Inupiat\'s subsistence uses
of bowhead whales"]{.underline}(Nelson, 2008, Sec II (B)(2)). [The
opinion of the case cites that **the "\[environmental assessment\]
itself admits** that 'it is unknown what the increased level of effect"
Shell's proposed exploration would create (Nelson, 2008).22 The
'petitioners', who "contend that \[some\] assumption is erroneous"
repeatedly are the opinions of the whaling captains that Itta submitted
because "there was a pretty long record indicating **they hadn't taken
any other suggestions into consideration**" (Nelson, 2008 and Interview
Mack, 2021).]{.underline} To get the lease approved after this
injunction, Slaiby (Interveiw, 2021) worked directly with Itta to ensure
that Shell "paid huge attention to traditional knowledge".23 Partnering
with Itta, Slaiby (Interview 2021) explains that since \"Edward had to
address TK in everything he did\...so did we\". Eventually, the two went
to DC to convince the federal government to approve Shell\'s offshore
activities.

[Demonstrating the empowerment of TK in Alaska, Andy Mack, CEO of Kuupik
Corporation, says, "if we come to the table with traditional knowledge,
it has equal value as whatever science \[outside companies\] bring"
(Interview Mack, 2021). He explains the implications of this stance when
negotiating benefit sharing with companies:]{.underline}

[1. Kuupik \"require a certain amount of sustainability and support that
\[they\] give to shareholders and\...to continue to develop and to value
\[TK\]\"]{.underline}

[2. TK is "one of the few tools that \[NCs\] have against a well-funded

about their wells,\...the subsurface\...costs of their project,
\[etc.\]"]{.underline}

Both Mack and Schutt are western trained lawyers and utilize TK in
negotiation "as a tool to protect subsistence by using \[TK\] to ensure
that \[development\] actually makes sense for us" (Interview Mack,
2021). For benefit sharing, TK "has great financial \[implications\]"
because outside companies "have to do everything they can to mitigate
and\...compensate" for the fact that "their activities are changing
incrementally and substantially over a period of time the

22 I noted that the opinion refers to the Alaska Natives as
"petitioners" and fails to give due credit to TK for identifying the
"gaps in \[the agency's\] data and the potential for serious

23 Bob Reiss recorded the story of Pete Slaiby and Edward Itta\'s

subsistence activities of" Alaska Natives (Interview Mack, 2021). Their
strategy strengthens the argument of this dissertation that outside
companies wanting to develop partnerships and make their operations more
sustainable should take seriously indigenous perspectives and expertise.

#### Certainty key\-\--overly technical jargon ruins the relationship and can muddle the goals

**McElhinny, Degawan, et al., 21** \[Vince, Minnie, Patricia Dunne, Alli

Arlington, VA: Conservation International.

Communities can offset the risk of project proponent interference by
demanding to know about possible conflicts of interest related to a
proposed expert, or choosing third-party experts themselves.
[Communities must be alert to whether the expert is providing
information that is in the community's best interest.]{.underline}
Non-Indigenous members of the negotiating team can play various roles,

building, or playing a role at the negotiation table. [The roles of

members will have already discussed the proposed project and its

### Plan\-\--1AC

#### Thus:

#### Plan: The United States federal government should significantly increase its development of the Arctic by authorizing new mineral resource extraction projects bound to benefit-sharing agreements co-designed and co-managed with Alaska Natives.

### Framing\-\--1AC

#### Contention 2: [FRAMING]{.underline}

#### Apocalyptic futurism launders colonial genocide --- white decline anxieties repackage structural violence as environmental crisis.

**Gergan** and Smith **20** Mabel Gergan \*Florida State University
\*\*and Pavithra Vasudevan University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill.
(Mabel & Sara, Earth beyond repair: Race and apocalypse in collective
imagination Society and Space 2020, Vol. 38(1) 91--110; KU Libraries
Sage Publications)//ishi

We argue that scientific [[debates]{.underline}]{.mark} and cultural
representations [offer]{.underline} parallel [**[imagining]{.mark}s of
[apocalypse]{.mark}** []{.mark}that **[escape]{.mark}**]{.underline}
specific **[[culpability]{.underline}]{.mark}** (for instance,
[[in]{.mark} processes of **[settler colonialism]{.mark}**]{.underline},
capitalism, or imperialism) [[and]{.mark} instead [center a **universal
human** ]{.mark}**frailty** that ends with triumph,]{.underline} a clear
moral, and a clean slate. Not all imaginaries reiterate this narrative;
in closing we turn to fantastical artwork that holds the violences of
colonialism, racism, and environmental destruction in tandem with
creative and abundant futures. Our aim, as three scholars working at the
intersection of climate justice, geopolitics, and futurity, is to bring
race more firmly into conversations of the Anthropocene. We suggest that
**[[apocalyptic imaginings]{.underline}]{.mark}** have often been framed
through an exclusionary hierarchy of humanity, necessitating closer
examination of how cliche´d genre conventions that saturate our media
environment [[rely on]{.mark} **anti-Black racism** and **[indigenous
erasure]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Without such attention, we risk
reiterating these cliche´s in narrating environmental crisis. We focus
on renderings of the apocalypse in American popular culture as a window
into cultural anxieties, following scholars in media studies and
ecocriticism (Murray and Heumann, 2014; O'Brien, 2016;
Willoquet-Maricondi, 2010) and geography (Dittmer and Gray, 2010; Dodds,
2008; Kennedy and Lukinbeal, 1997; Lukinbeal and Zimmermann, 2006;
Sharp, 1998). Bettini (2013) and Hartmann (2010) caution that unexamined
[[**apocalyptic imagery**]{.mark} in policy]{.underline} documents will
[[shift governance]{.underline}]{.mark} in response to climate change
[[from politics to security]{.underline}]{.mark}. The spillage of sci-fi
into science and security is not hypothetical; consider the U.S. Army's
recent "Mad Scientist Science Fiction Writing Contest," encouraging
contestants "to explore fresh ideas about the future of warfare and
technology\...with implications for how the Army operates in future
conflicts" (US Army Training and Doctrine Command, 2016). We write here
for political geographers, political ecologists, and scientists engaging
with the politics of the environmental future. It is beyond our
expertise to comprehensively detail genres of apocalypse, but rather we
outline three among many possible geo-historical junctures that can
clarify the political stakes of the Anthropocene. This does mean, as we
explain later, that our readings of the films lean into the cliche´s of
these genres, rather than untangle their simultaneous complexity. We
understand these geo-historical junctures as flashpoints of a
"master-narrative of the political unconscious" (Jameson, 1982), a
turbulent reckoning with what it means to be human that is fraught with
the racialized hauntings of genocide, slavery, and ongoing imperialism.
What kind of urgency does the Anthropocene produce? For whom? Baldwin
(2012: 172) argues [[whiteness]{.mark} is [figured through
"**tropes**]{.mark} **of**]{.underline} uncertainty, Utopia,
**[apocalypse]{.underline}**, prophesy, hope, fear, possibility and
potentiality." Here, we disaggregate futurity's tropes in conversation
with a parallel undoing of the fundamentally racialized definitions of
humanness (da Silva, 2011; Gilroy, 2018; Weheliye, 2014; Wynter, 2003).
At the core of processes that accelerated environmental devastation, now
represented as global, has been the consistent sacrifice of some lives
for the betterment of others. The Anthropocene uncomfortably reiterates
a nature/human binary figured in racialized terms, at times serving as a
proxy for deep-seated anxieties of racialized Others "taking over" the
planet. We construct three geo-historical junctures: a staged encounter
between geological proposals under review by the Anthropocene Working
Group and corresponding cinematic apocalyptic genres. Building on
Jameson's (1982: 153) articulation of sci-fi as dramatization of "our
incapacity to imagine the future\... the atrophy of the utopian
imagination," we analyze how these apocalyptic imaginaries break down or
intensify human/nature divisions in ways that sometimes disrupt but more
often reinvigorate a racial classification of humanity. We begin by
reviewing the Anthropocene as a collective contestation over what it
means to be human, elaborate our rationale for examining popular
culture, and then analyze the underlying racial premises of common
Hollywood tropes. We close the article with warning signs and alternate
imaginaries that disrupt this troubled legacy. Anthropocene as
apocalyptic futurity We build on a rich tradition of "storytelling" in
environmental justice activism and research (Houston and Vasudevan,
2017) that examines the Anthropocene as a narrative (Buell, 2014) whose
meaning is being contested among scientists, social movement actors,
critical theorists and cultural producers. What then do we learn from
the storytelling that takes place in debates over when the Anthropocene
began? Lewis and Maslin (2015, see also Davis and Todd, 2017) propose,
1610 as start date: the conquest of the Americas. This territorial
accumulation highlights, "a long-term and large-scale example of human
actions unleashing processes that are difficult to predict or manage"
(Lewis and Maslin, 2015: 177). While the, 1610 proposal has not gained
traction in the scientific community (Hamilton, 2015), its
acknowledgment of colonization's genocidal violence makes possible a
more incisive understanding of what is meant by the designation of the
epoch (Davis and Todd, 2017). Other proposals also highlight politically
charged moments in history, and are read as Gergan et al. 93 a
corrective to Enlightenment hubris (Lovbrand et al., 2009). As noted by
Robbins and € Moore (2013: 9), "Anthropocene scientific culture thus
simultaneously displays a panicked political imperative to intervene
more vocally and aggressively in an earth transformation run amok and an
increasing fear that past scientific claims about the character of
ecosystems and their transformation were overly normative, prescriptive,
or political in nature." Analyzing climate change as a literary
narrative explores how scientific knowledge gains traction,
"\[crystallizing\] the anxieties of a wider public" (Buell, 2014: 272).
For Yusoff (2013), this "geological turn" pushes our focus beyond social
relations with fossil fuels and human impacts on Earth, to think of
human being as itself geologically composed; the social then emerges as
an expression of geology and geochemistry. The Anthropocene debate
offers critical scholars a rare opportunity to engage with the
scientific community, making possible a more political geoscience
(Castree, 2015: 15). However, these proposals may elide who is contained
within the "human," while potentially legitimating "non-democratic and
technophilic approaches, such as geoengineering" (Baskin, 2015: 11).
Ahuja (2016) argues, "Geology is a spawn of the colonial capitalist
assemblage that is rapidly transforming the planet\... the discipline
cannot stand objectively outside the relations that term clumsily
attempts to name." We find ourselves in agreement with theorization of
the Capitalocene that challenges the narrative of a "fictitious human
unity" erasing the unevenness of ecological violence (Haraway, 2016;
Moore, 2017), and in agreement with critiques centering the persistent
role of colonial processes and settler colonialism as inseparable from
climate-driven conditions of violence (Davis and Todd, 2017; Whyte,
2016, 2013). For Baldwin (2014: 525), climate change anxieties reiterate
the human as a racial category "at a moment often characterized as
simultaneously post-racial and post-human." While post-humanist
scholarship has presented an important critique of the anthros,
postcolonial, decolonial and critical race studies suggest that analysis
of the Anthropocene must consider how colonial demarcations of the
human--nonhuman boundaries were premised upon and developed alongside
racial hierarchies of human difference (Gilroy, 2018; Jackson, 2013;
Livingstone et al., 2011). Ecological anxieties abound (Robbins and
Moore, 2013), and "fantasies of apocalypse are both a product and a
producer of the Anthropocene" (Ginn, 2015: 352). But what politics do
visions of future catastrophe engender (e.g. Baldwin, 2012; Ginn, 2015;
Katz, 1995; Schlosser, 2015; Skrimshire, 2010; Swyngedouw, 2013)?
Following the premise of popular geopolitics, we understand that popular
culture narratives enable particular forms of truth making, inciting
affective predispositions that generate political action (or inaction)
(Dittmer and Gray, 2010; Dodds, 2008; Sharp, 1998). Apocalyptic film can
be consumed as a spectacle of future ruin that closes space for
political action by fetishizing causes (such as carbon), proposing
technological fixes, and downplaying unevenly violent results of
ecological change. But it can also be a form of social dreaming that
makes different futures possible (Ginn, 2015; Schlosser, 2015). What
kind of present is generated by representations of the future that
reinscribe the racialized colonial origins of the Anthropocene? Future
rendered as "white decline" is fundamental to the operation of
biopolitical projects in the present (Smith and Vasudevan, 2017). This
anxiety is new in the specificity of Anthropocene as discourse, but also
contains strands of familiar apocalyptic imaginings, and crossover
between policy and pop culture. The cover of The Population Bomb
(Erhlich, 1968), centers a small white baby in a bomb to generate
anticipatory fear and action in the form of "population control." This
neoMalthusian vision is one among many cultural productions of its time
that reveal the undercurrent of fear under the guise of preventing war
and environmental catastrophe (Hartmann, 1995). But Public Enemy's, 1990
Fear of a Black Planet album cover art is, 94 EPD: Society and Space
38(1) to our eyes, a franker reading of overpopulation fears: a vision
of a Black planet eclipsing the earth, with Star Wars lettering and the
words "the counterattack on world supremacy" repeating at the bottom.
This cover art renders visible the submerged storylines of reversal and
takeover that occur through proxies and fantasy in mainstream pop
culture. White fragility (DiAngelo, 2011) is here stripped of pretense.
Geo-historical junctures and disasters as revelation As scholars sift
through the evidence to fix, subvert, or complicate the temporality of
the Anthropocene, their choice of markers echoes other imaginings of
disaster in revealing ways. In what follows, we trace webs of connection
between these geological markers and other imaginings of catastrophe, to
render visible the racial components of future-oriented anxiety in which
race is a fundamental but invisibilized mechanism of racial
neoliberalism (Baldwin, 2016; Goldberg, 2008). The determination of when
the Anthropocene began centers on potential "golden spikes" or Global
Stratotype Section and Point (GSSP) markers that signal global change --
that is, markers in glacier ice, in rock, or in sediment. Along with a
geological imprint, Crutzen and Stoermer (2000) argued that the proposed
marker should correspond to an important global historical juncture,
demonstrating how the choice of geological marker then also becomes part
of a political story about human agency, culpability, the past, and the
future (Lewis and Maslin, 2015). Here, we consider three geo-historical
junctures in conjunction with corresponding cultural anxieties. First,
Crutzen and Stoermer (2000) and subsequent studies (Waters et al., 2016)
locate the start date of the Anthropocene in the Industrial Revolution.
In Steffen et al.'s (2007) proposal, the Anthropocene is divided into
three stages: Industrial Era (1800-- 1945) marked by fossil fuel
expansion, the "Great Acceleration" (1945--2015) marked by rapid
industrialization and population growth, and the third stage "Stewards
of the Earth System" (2015 onwards), a speculative period marked by
growing awareness of humanity's impact on nature. In this narrative of
teleological humanism, strangely reminiscent of a crude variety of
Marxism minus the labor theory of value, we find humanity as
speciescoming-into-being as stewards of the earth. However, any hubris
around humanity as geologic force finds itself wrestling with reports of
dramatic shifts in weather patterns, melting ice caps, species
extinction, and other unpredictable ecological outcomes. We identify the
narrative of rising panic about climactic conditions or unpredictable
nature overtaking humanity as The Great Deluge. Second, we consider the
proposal (Lewis and Maslin, 2015; Zalasiewicz et al., 2015) marking the
accumulation of radioactivity in sediment from nuclear fallout. This
marker of the Anthropocene is also referred to as the "Bomb spike": the
peak in plutonium and artificial radionuclides from nuclear bomb
testing. The first atomic explosion was conducted in New Mexico in 1945,
followed by a peak in testing between the 1950s and early 1960s. Lewis
and Maslin (2015) propose 1964 as the start date of this marker, because
of the distinct, measurable peak in atmospheric radiocarbon. This marker
not only refers to nuclear testing, but also the period since the 1950s
labeled The Great Acceleration, characterized by an intensification of
industrial and technological development of materials capable of
destroying human and ecological life. While nuclear testing was
celebrated as American techno-scientific might, for Lewis and Maslin
(2015: 177) this is a "story of an elite-driven technological
development that threatens planet-wide destruction." We identify this
proliferation of toxic and molecular nonhuman agents as Nuclear
Cataclysm. Third, we consider the proposal (Lewis and Maslin, 2015) that
the Anthropocene originates in 1610, marked by a dramatic increase in
carbon sequestration due to reforestation Gergan et al. 95 following the
colonization of the Americas (Lewis and Maslin, 2015). We map this
proposal onto the recurring trope of The Population Bomb and anxieties
about racialized populations. Lewis and Maslin (2015: 174) define the
"Orbis Spike," as follows: "The arrival of Europeans in the Caribbean in
1492, and subsequent annexing of the Americas, led to the largest human
population replacement in the past 13,000 years, the first global trade
networks linking Europe, China, Africa and the Americas, and the
resultant mixing of previously separate biotas, known as the Columbian
Exchange." This "exchange," led to an unfathomable decline: with an
estimated beginning population of between 54 and 61 million people being
reduced to 6 million within less than 200 years due to war, disease,
enslavement, and famine. This devastation left its mark as the
regeneration of an estimated 50 million hectares of forest led to
increased carbon uptake and a decline in atmospheric CO2 that in turn
was documented in Antarctic ice core records. Both Lewis & Maslin's
proposals and these films are warnings: they work to generate an
affective reaction and relationship to the environment with a specific
political intent. Here, we build on media analysis traditions in
geography and ecocriticism, to outline particular genres of
future-oriented cultural production (mainly films, but also science
fiction, art, television, music videos, and animation). We approach
these genres and particular exemplars as specific forms of apocalypse.
We are grateful to one anonymous reviewer who pointed to the dual
meaning of the term, apocalypse both as "a disaster resulting in
drastic, irreversible damage to human society or the environment,
especially on a global scale," and as "revelation" or "disclosure"
(Oxford English Dictionary Online, n.d.). Drawing on this understanding
of apocalypse as disasters that reveal, our approach has been to comb
through pervasive representations of apocalypse (here, mainly US)
popular culture, and outline the recurrent tropes that cohere into
specific genres. We began with an open field and developed a list of
cliche´d tropes of disaster films that contain an element of
futureorientation and generated box office success (we excluded, for
instance, Twister and alieninvasion films). Here, we created a broad
taxonomy that we hope to elaborate on in future work: nature's revenge
films, here captured in "The Great Deluge," films that evoke fears of
nuclear power or weapons gone awry, here designated as "Nuclear
Cataclysm," and zombie and contagion-based films that evoke
population-related fears, here glossed as "The Population Bomb." For the
Great Deluge and Population Bomb, we discuss the genre broadly, but then
focus on The Day after Tomorrow, and World War Z, respectively, which
are the highest lifetime grossing films of the two genres. For fears of
Nuclear Cataclysm, our task was complicated as we noticed a sharp rise
in films corresponding to Cold War geopolitics, and then a striking
absence after the 1980s, replaced by the genre of irradiated
Superheroes. We suggest that the absence of nuclear fears is itself
revealing of the uneven and racialized distribution of nuclear risk. We
want to emphasize that there are nuances in some of these individual
films that are well worth exploring, but that the scope of our argument
and limited space here confines us to much broader brushstrokes. We push
to consider the political implications of designating the starting point
of the Anthropocene by uncovering the meanings that inform the cultural
context within which these decisions are made, with special attention to
the "revelation," and "disclosure," of racialized evacuation of
responsibility or designation of culpability. This approach has led us
to three genres that for us form geo-historical junctures: collisions
between scientific reasoning of the Anthropocene and cultural fears that
are embedded in future imaginaries. Lukinbeal and Zimmermann (2006)
argue that cinematic technologies are embedded in sociocultural
practices, and that science fiction is particularly revealing (Jameson,
1982). How have films primed their audiences to interpret the future in
ways that both reflect and prefigure policy responses and ontological
assumptions about the ongoing and impending 96 EPD: Society and Space
38(1) effects of Anthropocene living? In the following section, we think
through the archive of existent cultural representations of future
catastrophe, and the eerie echoes that link cinematic cultural worlds
with the geologic era. In this, we find that apocalypse films, intended
as forms of revelation, often unwittingly reveal something other than
intended. The great deluge Fears of an ecological catastrophe fueled by
industrialization and overconsumption are reflected in the growing genre
of climate fiction or "cli-fi" (Dan Bloom, cited in Svoboda, 2016).
Whether they take the form of a hurricane full of sharks in Sharknado,
rising seas in Waterworld, synchronized hailstorms, tornadoes and
blizzards in The Day After Tomorrow (TDAT) or the eruption of the
earth's core in 2012, cli-fi films portend impending disaster where
nature takes revenge on an unwitting human population that realizes its
mistakes too late. On the surface, cli-fi films challenge climate
inaction by sensationalizing extreme events, a liberal Hollywood version
of Al Gore's charge to accept the "inconvenient truth" of climate
change. However, erasing the work of earlier generations of
eco-apocalyptic fables, such as Carson's Silent Spring which inspired a
generation of environmental activism, cli-fi obfuscates the connections
between climate change and structural conditions in a battle of
(triumphant survivor) Man v. (vengeful uncontrollable) Nature. In
cli-fi, disaster becomes another storyline for proclaiming white
victory. The predominant tendency in cli-fi is the representation of the
apocalypse as "The Great Deluge": a real or figurative wave that
threatens to overwhelm all humanity in climate events of biblical
proportions. TDAT is said to have generated more than ten times the news
coverage than the, 2001 IPCC report, and had a significant impact on
public understanding of climate change (Lieserowitz, 2004). At the
center of the story is a paleoclimatologist who draws attention to an
imminent Ice Age but is ignored by the powers that be. Superstorms wreak
havoc, and the scientist must save his trapped son in New York City. At
the end, the new Ice Age arrives, covering the northern hemisphere in
ice and snow, signaling that few survived except a small (mostly white)
resilient band. While TDAT was critiqued by many climatologists for
indulging in sensationalism and undermining scientific research, the
movie portrays the scientific community as morally unfettered to a
corrupt establishment. Here, scientists stand in for stewards of the
earth, who are charged with both a moral authority and responsibility, a
sentiment also reflected in Steffen et al.'s (2007) final stage of the
Anthropocene. This stage speculates how geo-engineering efforts might
offer solutions to our destructive human impact. This framing of science
as savior obscures how a scientific rationality laid the foundation for
Enlightenment thinking and the Industrial Revolution (Lovbrand et al.,
2009). € The "Great Deluge" trope retrenches inherited categories of
"human" versus "nature" implicit in the Anthropocene. Matthews and
Simpson (2014: 22), note that cli-fi "presents an unexpectedly feral,
unpredictable environment where an aggressive nature runs rampant." Even
if such stories begin with a critique of overconsumption, mid-way
through the narrative, the causal arrow points squarely at a vengeful
nature that must be subdued by humans. In TDAT, (as in World War Z,
below), the stand in for "human" is a white middle-aged man, evoking the
white savior trope and the scientist/savior figure (Murray and Heumann,
2014). As Tuck and Ree (2013: 640) note, mainstream (horror) US films
are "preoccupied with the hero, who is perfectly innocent, but who is
assaulted by monstering or haunting just the same." TDAT invests white
characters with scientific authority, as in one scene where a Black
police officer ignores the white lead's warnings, a mistake that
ultimately costs his and others' lives (McGreavy and Lindenfeld, 2014:
124). As climate change is declared a global Gergan et al. 97 threat,
white maleness stands in for a universal human subject and the first
sites to be destroyed are located in the northern hemisphere. On a more
fundamental level, the threat to "humanity" represented in Great Deluge
films are perhaps expressing what Baldwin (2016: 84) terms a white
affect, a "pre-discursive intensity that forms when white positionality
confronts the fantasy of its own death." For a growing number of people,
however, extreme weather events are not a dystopian future but a grim
reality, while simultaneously, the presumed threat of "climate refugee"
incursions is used to effect a securitization that targets migrants
themselves (Ahuja, 2016; Bettini, 2013; Farbotko and Lazrus, 2012;
Hartmann, 2010). Unlike the protagonists of TDAT, for those actually
facing climate change, "The world is sectioned into nationals and
nationalities for those who cannot afford to move or travel beyond their
home countries. For the rich, the world is indeed transnational and
deterritorialized" (Miyoshi, 2001: 292). The flip side of these films is
the desire for a security apparatus reliant on technological innovation
and governance. Through the construction of a seemingly universal
post-racial apocalypse, Great Deluge films suggest "the guarantee of
white supremacy lies in its capacity to contain the excess" (Baldwin,
2016: 84) of climate change's consequences. In films like TDAT, risks
posed by climate change are displaced onto others who are seen as
lacking the resourcefulness of the survivor and viewer. This deferral of
disaster to the future erases the ways in which climate change already
affects livelihoods (differentially). The urgency of apocalypse promotes
a temporality of anticipated white precarity belying the root causes of
the Anthropocene in the longue dure´e of racial capitalism. While
Hollywood movies in general center white male characters, cli-fi films
portrayal of a universal human threat through the experience of a white
Western core is at odds with realities of disparate vulnerability
articulated by climate justice movements (e.g. Chatterton et al., 2013;
Pettit, 2004; Pulido, 2012). This critique also draws our attention to
the uneven geopolitics of climate change and science (Lukinbeal and
Zimmermann, 2006), and how humanity has been defined in violent
opposition against Black people (Weheliye, 2014). Nuclear cataclysm In
analyzing the nuclear cataclysm genre alongside the "Bomb spike," a
radioactive isotope marker, the geopolitical context of this era becomes
quite significant. Both the film genre and the marker appear in the
aftermath of World War II where the hardening of borders and ideologies
was accompanied by the re-interpretation of terms such as "nation,"
"civilization," and "race" (Williams, 2011). In the context of the Cold
War, as former colonies declared their independence and challenged
Euro-Western imperialism, nuclear dominance stood in for scientific and
racial superiority. However, alongside triumphalist narratives of
scientific might, 1950s films articulated a growing fear that nuclear
technology could turn on its makers. In one of the first nuclear monster
movies, Them! (1954), we find eight-foot long irradiated ants discovered
in New Mexico after the Trinity tests. The same year, the cult classic
Japanese film, Gojira was released, later remade for American audiences
as Godzilla: King of the monsters. Noriega (1987: 68) notes the
difference in the Japanese and American versions; the former
symbolically repeats the trauma of Hiroshima, and the latter in its
destruction of Godzilla, seeks to "repress\[es\] American guilt and
anxieties about nuclear weapons." Sontag (1966; cited in Anisfield,
1995: 55) believes that the destruction wreaked by these monsters also
stimulated a cathartic moment for audiences, relieving them of their
nuclear anxiety and guilt by allowing them to participate in the fantasy
of "living through one's own death and more, the death of cities, the
destruction of humanity itself." In the 1950s, mutant monsters, whether
evolved from radiation or accidentally awakened 98 EPD: Society and
Space 38(1) from pre-historic slumber by nuclear testing, signaled a
growing fear of nuclear cataclysm as imminent threat. The Three-Mile
incident in Pennsylvania (1979) and Chernobyl disaster (1986) shifted
public fears from abstraction to reality, with films like The China
Syndrome (1979) and Silkwood (1983) featuring hyper-realistic
interpretations of nuclear war and contamination. The Day After (1983),
a made for television film, is perhaps the most well remembered, a film
that so affected Ronald Reagan he reconsidered his nuclear proliferation
plans (Gault, 2015). Set in Kansas, the film presents a full-scale
nuclear attack and its aftermath. As fears are building, a central
character commenting about the imminent destruction says, "Hiroshima was
peanuts." In dark irony, the film utilized declassified film and
photographs depicting the actual Hiroshima bombing; following this
four-minute long scene, the film manifests a bleak post-nuclear
landscape where annihilation is more desirable than survival. Mixed in
with fears of revenge exacted by racial others, or irradiated monsters,
was the awful possibility of "civilization" being blown back to a
primitive era. The film ends with a warning: "The catastrophic events
you have just witnessed are, in all likelihood, less severe than the
destruction that would actually occur in the event of a full nuclear
strike against the United States\..." Following Arundhati Roy's
injunction that nuclear weapons are "white weapons," Williams (2011)
argues that nuclear cataclysms in science fiction has always been
embedded with racial undertones, reflecting white moral and
technological superiority and its destructive recklessness. As the Cold
War ended, nuclear fears were gradually replaced in popular cultural
imaginary with mutant superheroes like the Teenage Mutant Ninja Turtles,
who present an alternate story of mutation and radiation (Falkof, 2013).
In the trustworthy hands of Master Splinter and the ninja turtles,
radioactive waste could be used for good. In the leap from edgy comic
book to children's television, the turtles' mutation is never unsettling
or threatening, and is instead "superseded by the positive aspects of
their other traits \[teenagers who love pizzas and also happen to be
ninjas\]" (Falkof, 2013: 46). Nuclear force no longer engenders fear,
guilt, or anxiety in the viewer, sanitizing consequences of rampant and
uncontrolled nuclear power. That the mainstream incorporation of TMNT
has comedic elements and is produced for children marks the degree to
which this genre has undergone a radical transformation from earlier
iterations. Despite active concerns of nuclear waste disposal, ageing
reactors, and contamination in the eventuality of extreme events like
Fukushima, we find a notable absence of the Nuclear Cataclysm genre
after the 1980s. Even as we consider new representations of nuclear
technology, the question lingers, why has the Nuclear Cataclysm genre
faded and what has it been replaced with instead? To some extent,
American films have sublimated and redirected nuclear fears to terrorism
(Dodds, 2008). Moreover, global warming has led to a renewed optimism
around nuclear energy. Reporting on the ongoing cleanup efforts at
Fukushima, Miles O'Brien argues that disasters gave nuclear power a
dangerous reputation, but asks, "Is nuclear the villain here, or is it
inattention to iterating and improving the technology?" (Staff, 2017).
In this we find echoes of Steffen et al.'s (2007) final "Stewards of the
Earth System" stage of the Anthropocene and its belief that ecological
calamities can be averted with technophilic solutions. Optimism around
nuclear technology masks the sobering reality of toxic legacies of
uranium mining, processing, and waste disposal siting in the U.S, that
have most severely impacted indigenous populations and racialized
minorities from the U.S. Marshall Islands to the Navajo Nation and
Alaska (Houston, 2013; Kyne and Bolin, 2016). Given the racialized
burden of nuclear testing and siting -- an ongoing apocalypse -- its
absence in American popular consciousness is telling but perhaps
unsurprising. Gergan et al. 99 The population bomb The "Orbis Spike,"
binds white North American settler colonialism to catastrophe and
existential ecological crisis. However, in the eco-narratives that haunt
both putatively environmental policies of population control and
Hollywood fantasies, destructive invaders are figured quite differently.
The Population Bomb (Erhlich, 1968) asked on its cover: "Population
control or race to oblivion?" Neo-Malthusian fears imagine a
fundamentally Other, hungry, and unmanageable population: an untamable
horde. In 1973's Soylent Green, the future brings a dehumanized urban
mass fighting for green crackers\...the origin of which is ominously
revealed: "Soylent Green is people!" In a cannibalistic echo,
Snowpiercer (2013) takes us to 2031: on this dystopian train that serves
as a parable of economic class, food for the oppressed is made of
cockroaches, but small children are used as the train's replacement
parts. Baldwin's (2016) analysis of the climate migrant opens with a
description of a London art exhibit: Postcards from the future, in which
laundry hangs from landmarks, Jaipur monkeys clamber, and a squatter
settlement sprawls in front of Buckingham Palace. Race is not named, but
these images "scream race: the icons of Britishness, of whiteness\...
overrun by hordes of climate migrants" (Baldwin, 2016: 80; citing
Goldberg, 2008). We argue that white affect, entangled with fears of a
Black/brown population bomb, is sublimated and submerged by the
dehumanizing consumption of humans as food (Soylent Green), as machinery
(Snowpiercer), or as producers of life (Mad Max: Fury Road), in airports
as ominous vectors of extinction-threatening disease in films like
Contagion and Rise of Planet of the Apes, and through the lumbering
zombies of World War Z (WWZ), as we examine below. While emerging from
Afro-Caribbean cultural traditions and deeply tied to the violence of
slavery, refracted through cinema the zombie emerged as a different kind
of monster that could stand in for a range of horrors (McAlister, 2012;
McIntosh and Leverette, 2008; Schlosser, 2017; Strauss, 2015). McAlister
(2012: 461; see also Sheller, 2003) observes that the "the zombie
represents, responds to, and mystifies fear of slavery, collusion with
it, and rebellion against it." In Hollywood, zombies shift from being
racialized as mostly Black in the 1930s and 1940s to mostly white,
mindless stand-ins for the sins of consumerist and militaristic
Americans in the films of George Romero (1968's Night of the Living
Dead, and 1978's Dawn of the Dead); in Romero's films, human heroes are
often Black, while zombies are "Hyperwhites" (Bishop, 2008; McAlister,
2012; Schlosser, 2017). Read as a threat to the freedom of the private
self (May, 2010); as reaction to economic crisis (Fojas, 2017); as
response to terrorism (Bishop, 2009); and as subaltern monster
threatening colonial order through its postcolonial positioning (Bishop,
2008), zombies embody the edge of alterity (May, 2010; Saunders, 2012).
We follow Saunders (2012) in viewing the current zombie turn as newly
geopolitical and tied to fears of existential threat, but also maintain,
with McAlister (2012) and Bishop (2008), that the zombie is complexly
embedded in allegories of colonization. Schlosser (2017) posits zombies
as fear of the loss of individual freedom to collectivity; we find these
analyses of zombies to be commensurable with the framing of the white
protagonist facing off against the hordes. WWZ, the highest grossing
zombie movie in history, began as a novel written by Max Brooks, who was
inspired by Studs Terkel and by Romero's zombie movies (NPR Staff,
2012). The 2013 film departs wildly from the Brooks novel, and was
developed by actor Brad Pitt and director Marc Forster after they failed
to make a movie about AIDS (Holson, 2013). The film places Pitt as
Gerry, a savvy UN operative who must save the world. The central white
male character overcomes untenable odds through a potent mix of rational
100 EPD: Society and Space 38(1) and justified violence. He is motivated
to save "humanity," represented by his innocent white wife and
daughters, thus placing the reunification of the heteronormative white
family at the center of the drama (Fojas, 2017). The film repeatedly
reinforces the point that connection is danger and people can become
nonhuman: the opening credits splice images of people on their way to
work, silly talk shows, teeming ants, and wolves fighting and tearing at
prey. Absent Romero's critiques of race-relations and consumerism, or
the culturally rooted connection to slavery of the original zombie
mythology, the zombies of WWZ are an ideal proxy for the horde. Zombies
comprise teeming masses whose form remains identifiably human, but
unrecognizably monstrous -- in the terms of Tuck and Ree (2013, in
reference to Haraway), this is "making-killable." Within the film-world,
the deployment of violence, unfettered by moral concerns, celebrates
securitized settler colonialism enacted by the white protagonist through
a cunning mix of science, cool wits, and violence. Each site in the
film-world entrenches colonial geopolitics of security and control, but
here, the most revealing site of epidemic is Israel. An elaborate
conversation about the 1967 war justifies Israel as the most proactive
in taking steps to securitize their borders. Jerusalem's security wall
thus protects against a horde of dehumanized zombies that pile upon one
another like ants gone mad while they are attacked from the air. A brief
moment of Israelis and Palestinians singing together in cooperation
drives the zombies into a frenzy, leading to an irreparable breach as
zombies spill over the wall and wreak devastation. After infecting
himself with a deadly virus to become invisible to zombies, Pitt paves
the way for the WHO to begin implementing a vaccine program, while
people flee north to settle in for war. WWZ suggests that (militarized)
science is magic, and that connections between places lead to contagion
and dehumanization. A resilient white protagonist facing zombie hordes
and pestilence threatening human extermination takes on a bitterly
ironic resonance when put in relation to the conquest of the Americas.
Hinging between white settler colonialism's actual work of elimination
and pestilence (Stannard, 1993) and zombie and contagion narratives'
imagined dehumanization, decline, and illness, we see an epic reversal,
in which biopolitical mechanisms of white futurity are shored up by a
profoundly duplicitous representation of who is threatened and who is
threat. In WWZ, the mechanisms of settler colonialism are celebrated:
suspicion is heroic, walls enable survival, quarantine is necessary.
This ironic revisioning is one way that "white affect" (Baldwin, 2016)
rescripts historical events through fantasies of its own future death.
Outside Hollywood, this reversal emerges in policies that rewrite those
subordinated by structural oppression as threats to security and the
environment, or as helpless but dangerous victims (Bettini, 2013;
Farbotko and Lazrus, 2012; Hartmann, 2010; Sundberg, 2007). Descent into
chaos is mystified to promote support for classist and racist measures,
technological solutions, and security. Warning signs: How not to survive
an apocalypse By tracing how Anthropocene markers evoke apocalypse, we
complicate the "newness" of the Anthropocene, unravel threads that bind
it to racialized threat, and identify [the geopolitics of [apocalyptic
thinking]{.mark}]{.underline} that [[**perpetuate colonial** and
**imperial destruction**]{.underline}]{.mark}. Anthropocene discourse
reflects Enlightenment thinking but also an attempt to redress it; that
is, it reflects the problematic centrality of humans and a
human/nonhuman binary even as it seeks to undo and problematize that
logic and its destructive effects. Can we preserve the urgency of the
Anthropocene without re-deploying destructive understandings of
humans/nonhumans? Following Goldberg (2008), Weheliye (2014), Baldwin
(2016), and others, we have sought to make visible how the future is
carelessly (or intentionally) reiterative of the Enlightenment
human/non-human demarcation that justified planetary colonization by
white Europeans, engendering current crises. In working through the
above analyses, we have identified cinematic tropes that double as
warning signs -- the points at which you shout at the screen: "Watch
out! You're in danger!" [As markers of white affect]{.underline}, they
further the differential **[[apocalyptic]{.underline}]{.mark}** outcomes
of the Anthropocene that reiterate death-dealing colonial violence. We
frame these as **[[warnings]{.underline}]{.mark}**, because they
[[emerge]{.underline}]{.mark} not only in films about mutant turtles and
zombies, but also [[in]{.mark} **scholarship** and
**[policy]{.mark}**]{.underline}. When we encounter these, we suggest
looking back at what came before, looking around to see who is included,
and looking ahead, to examine what future is prefigured. Discussed in
detail below, the five warning signs are: (1) temporal displacement of
crisis into the future, (2) humans versus the horde, (3) the epic
reversal in which the oppressed become the oppressor, (4) science is
magic, and (5) the absence. Our first warning sign is "The apocalypse is
coming (the crisis has just begun)." [We [recommend **skepticism**
