enacted]{.mark} by the One Big Beautiful Bill Act]{.underline}, which
Interior will be implementing through an expedited rulemaking process:
**[[Offshore oil and gas development]{.mark}]{.underline}** The federal
[[royalty rate]{.underline}]{.mark} is reduced to [[no less than
12.5%]{.mark} and no greater than 16.67%.]{.underline} This change
[gives the Department of the Interior increased discretion to [encourage
domestic]{.mark} Outer Continental Shelf [production and
investment]{.mark} by lowering the royalty rate charged to
producers.]{.underline} In 2022, [the Inflation Reduction
Act]{.underline} mandated a 16.67% minimum royalty rate, [discouraging
investment in domestic oil and gas production]{.underline}. In the Gulf
of America, the Bureau of Ocean Energy Management is mandated to hold at
least two offshore lease sales each year through 2039. The first of
these lease sales is scheduled on December 10, 2025. The second of these
lease sales will be held by March 15, 2026, and the third will be held
by August 15, 2026. [**[Alaska leasing resumes]{.mark}** with a
**[minimum of six offshore lease sales]{.mark}** occurring in the Cook
Inlet Planning Area Leasing from 2026-2032.]{.underline} The first of
these lease sales will be held by March 15, 2026. Under the One Big
Beautiful Bill Act, Bureau of Safety and Environmental Enforcement, or
BSEE, must approve commingling requests unless safety or resource
recovery would be harmed. Downhole commingling is the production of oil
or natural gas from multiple reservoirs through a single well. The
process allows for more efficient and cost-effective production. Onshore
oil and gas development The royalty rate for new federal onshore
production will also return to a minimum 12.5%, reversing the 16.67%
rate established in the Inflation Reduction Act. This royalty rate
reduction will [lead to an estimated 225 additional leases in
2026]{.underline}, and over the following few years, will lead to an
average of 160 wells drilled on these additional leases. [Onshore lease
sales]{.underline} will be [required quarterly in]{.underline} Wyoming,
New Mexico, Colorado, Utah, Montana, North Dakota, Oklahoma, Nevada and
[Alaska]{.underline}. The One Big Beautiful Bill Act eliminates
expression of interest fees, paid by lessees to nominate land for
leasing, also an unnecessary measure pushed by Democrats in the
Inflation Reduction Act to restrict new energy production Upon approval,
a permit to drill will now be valid for a four-year period. The royalty
requirement is repealed on extracted methane established by the
Inflation Reduction Act, which recklessly forced producers to pay
extremely burdensome fees or to shut-in production. The Bureau of Land
Management, or BLM, must now lease nominated parcels within 18 months
and may not add new restrictions outside of the conditions of approval
included in the resource management plans. New legal definitions for
"eligible" and "available" lands are being adopted to reduce leasing
disputes. [The [BLM]{.mark} will [update]{.mark} oil and gas
[regulations that]{.mark} would make it [easier]{.mark} for operators to
combine, or commingle, production [from multiple leases]{.mark}. This
measure reduces costs, improves production and decreases surface
use.]{.underline} Coal The royalty rate will be reduced from 12.5% to
7%, which significantly decreases the per-ton cost of producing coal,
improving financial viability for producers. Federal leasing will be
restored, including a mandate to increase the availability for lease of
4 million acres of public lands with known coal reserves. Coal lease
reviews will be streamlined. Water Infrastructure and Hydropower 3/3 The
Bureau of Reclamation will adopt an accelerated permitting process for
major water infrastructure projects, including dams, canals and
reservoirs, by allowing Reclamation to use the voluntary National
Environmental Policy Act fee system. Access to hydropower development
will be expanded on federal dams, with streamlined licensing and
priority for water storage projects that include power generation.
Reclamation will be able to expedite review of Title XVI water recycling
projects and desalination feasibility studies, removing certain previous
cost caps. Reclamation is directed to issue guidance on leveraging
public-private partnerships for infrastructure upgrades, including
drought resilience and water reuse systems. The One Big Beautiful Bill
Act allocates \$1 billion to the Bureau of Reclamation through 2034 for
construction and associated activities that restore or increase the
capacity or use of existing conveyance facilities or for construction
that increases the capacity of existing surface water storage
facilities. Forestry and Public Lands The BLM will expand timber sales,
increasing by 20 million board feet annually. Furthermore, the BLM will
prioritize long-term timber contracts and open additional harvests. In
addition to the actions above, the Department will address provisions in
the One Big Beautiful Bill Act to eliminate longstanding right-of-way
and capacity fee discounts for existing and future wind and solar
projects, bringing an end to years of subsidies for economically
unviable energy development. From boosting American energy production
and revitalizing the clean coal sector, to accelerating domestic mineral
development, expanding timber harvesting and cutting burdensome
regulations, the Department of the Interior---under Secretary Burgum's
leadership---is delivering results and advancing President Trump's bold
agenda through the One Big Beautiful Bill. [[The BLM manages]{.mark}
about [245 million acres]{.mark} of public land located primarily in 12
western states, [including Alaska]{.mark}]{.underline}, on behalf of the
American people. The BLM also administers 700 million acres of
sub-surface mineral estate throughout the nation. Our mission is to
sustain the health, diversity, and productivity of America's public
lands for the use and enjoyment of present and future generations.

### UQ\-\--Corporations Say Yes\-\--2AC

#### Oil companies said yes -- empirics prove

Maria S. **Tysiachniouk and** Andrey N. **Petrov**, 05-xx-20**18**,
\[Maria S. Tysiachniouk Environmental Policy Group, Wageningen
University, Centre for Independent Social Research\] \[Andrey N. Petrov,
arctic center and Department of Geography, University of Northern
Iowa\], Benefit sharing in the Arctic energy sector: Perspectives on
corporate policies and practices in Northern Russia and Alaska, No
Publication,
https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S2214629617303420,
accessed 7-24-2025, Eric Cardman

Since the last century, the Arctic has become an important arena for
energy resource extraction, and this activity is expected to grow in the
decades to come \[\[1\], \[2\]\]. [Many transnational energy companies
([TNC]{.mark}) are actively [engaged in]{.mark} the exploration and
development of [oil and natural gas reserves]{.mark} in the high
latitudes, and are facing serious policy challenges in respect to
dealing with the local communities and state actors]{.underline}. Oil
and natural gas extraction in remote regions, including the Arctic,
brings opportunities for development, but also inflicts costs to local
communities and Indigenous peoples. It affects the subsistence economy
and removes land from traditional resource use. The costs of resource
extraction to local communities may outweigh the benefits, which, in
turn, affect the social and environmental security in the Arctic \[3\].
[The [majority of oil and gas companies in the Arctic]{.mark} have
[declared]{.mark} their [commitment to benefit-sharing
arrangements]{.mark} that [assist]{.mark} local and [Indigenous
communities and]{.mark} protect local and [Indigenous rights to land and
traditional resources]{.mark}]{.underline} \[4\]. Benefit sharing
generally refers to an [[exchange]{.mark} between actors granting
[access to]{.mark} a particular [resource and]{.mark} actors providing
[compensation]{.mark} or reward for its use]{.underline} \[5\], as well
as the [distribution of the [monetary and non-monetary]{.mark} benefits
produced by a resource-based project \[]{.underline}6\]. The
implementation of these commitments varies considerably among the
regions, companies, and communities. Large surveys of literature have
been undertaken in respect to benefit sharing in mining industry (see
\[\[7\], \[8\], \[9\]\]), including remote regions \[\[10\], \[11\],
\[12\], \[13\]\], but only recently the discussion has evolved to focus
on the energy extractive sector in the Arctic and sub-Arctic regions
\[\[14\], \[15\], \[16\], \[17\], \[18\]\]. The analysis and
systematization of Arctic experiences is still in its beginning stages,
although there is enough empirical material and case studies to present
a classification and initial assessment of the benefit sharing modes and
policy models, as we attempt below. The review of benefit sharing
arrangements in different Arctic countries and regions is based on our
field case studies in Russia and Alaska, as well as, on literature
review. Field work took place 2014--2017 in Nenets Autonomous District,
Khanti-Mansi Autonomous District, Sakhalin Island, Irkutsk Region, and
the North Slope of Alaska. Qualitative methodology using semi-structured
interviews, participant observations, and document analysis has been
used. In addition, literature on the topic of benefit sharing has been
analysed. Research indicates that there is seemingly no ideal blueprint,
or a single set of best practices leading to benefit sharing conditions
satisfactory to local and Indigenous-nomadic communities. By examining
the typology of benefit sharing governance modes and corresponding
company policy models, we argue that some modes are more advantageous
for meeting community needs and fostering sustainable development in
remote regions. Concurrently, we explain why, despite
company-community-state cooperation dating back to at least the 1970s,
we have not seen the emergence of a successful benefit sharing model. We
analyse what elements of Alaskan and Russian experiences could be used
to build more locally-responsive and sustainable Arctic benefit sharing
frameworks. Finally, we present our policy perspective and suggest new
research directions to fill existing knowledge and policy gaps.

#### subsistence harvesting k2 culture

A. An Inuit Framework for Food Security and Food Sovereignty

In 2020, the ICC Alaska published Food Sovereignty and Self-Governance:
Inuit Role in Managing Arctic Marine Resources. The 144-page report
documents resource management in Inuit communities in Alaska and the
Inuvialuit Settlement Region of Canada. [It analyzes the legislative and
regulatory structures that govern Inuit food security and evaluates
existing co-management schemes. Moreover, the report offers a framework
for understanding food security rooted in Indigenous Knowledge, using
theoretical tools devised by and for Inuit. The ICC Alaska framework
recognizes that [food security]{.mark} involves **[more than material
subsistence]{.mark}** because [food-based practices]{.mark} are [bound
up with]{.mark} **[spirituality, linguistics, knowledge, environmental
health, and self-governance]{.mark}**.]{.underline}33 The report
ultimately asserts that food security requires ["food sovereignty,"
[defined as:]{.mark}]{.underline}

[the [right]{.mark} of all Inuit [to define]{.mark} their [own hunting,
gathering, fishing, land, and water policies;]{.mark} the [right to
define]{.mark} [what is]{.mark} [sustainably, socially, economically,
and culturally appropriate for the distribution of food and to maintain
ecological health;]{.mark} and the [right to obtain and maintain
practices that ensure]{.mark} access to tools needed to [obtain,
process, store, and consume traditional foods.]{.mark}]{.underline}34

While ICC Alaska uses the language of rights to describe the concept of
food sovereignty, structurally, [the distinction between food security
and food sovereignty involves power. [Food sovereignty ensures food
security]{.mark} insofar as it [requires Native control of Native
foods,]{.mark} which [in turn requires control over resources and
land.]{.mark}]{.underline}35

[This notion of food sovereignty stands apart from Alaska's existing
resource management strategies, including models developed by
progressive management researchers and regulators who embrace the
rhetoric of co-management and Indigenous Knowledge.]{.underline} As Part
III details, [academic and professional discussions of subsistence
rights and resource management feature a limited space for change,
restricted by technocratic and colonial assumptions. These restrictive
visions of co-management seek to fit Indigenous consultants and
Indigenous Knowledge into the existing American management
framework.]{.underline} They fail to imagine a real transfer of
decision-making power to Natives, let alone management regimes
structurally informed by Native worldviews. To achieve the ICC Alaska
notion of food sovereignty, the discourse of co-management must be
upset.

#### another k2 culture card 

Krista M. **Heeringa et al** 20**19** \[Krista M. Heeringa 1, Orville
Huntington 2, Brooke Woods 2, F. Stuart Chapin III 1, Richard E. Hum 1,
1: University of Alaska Fairbanks, 2: Tanana Chiefs Conference,
10-25-2019, "A Holistic Definition of Healthy Traditional Harvest
Practices for Rural Indigenous Communities in Interior Alaska," Journal
of Agriculture, Food Systems, and Community Development,
https://www.foodsystemsjournal.org/index.php/fsj/article/view/750,
accessed 7-21-2025 // ava :)\]

Climate Change Presents New Challenges

In addition to economic, social, and political change, accelerating
environmental changes associ-ated with climate warming add a layer of
complex-ity and vulnerability to the harvesting of wild foods (Brinkman,
Hansen, Chapin, Kofinas, BurnSilver, & Rupp, 2016). Climate assessments
have shown warming in the Arctic that is about twice the global average
(Chapin et al., 2014, Comiso & Hall, 2014). Indigenous communities
experience impacts from this warming in a number of ways. For example,
frozen rivers are important travel corridors for hunting, trapping, and
wood harvesting. Warmer winters have led to later freeze-up and earlier
break-up of rivers in addition to longer time that rivers are unsafe to
travel on, thus inhibiting access to harvest resources (Brown, Brinkman,
Verbyla, Brown, Cold, & Hollingsworth, 2018). These changes influence
not only human movement but also the migration of wildlife, such as
caribou, on which communities rely (Leblond, St-Laurent, & Côté, 2016).

[In the past, reliable environmental cues such as the timing of the
seasons and consistency in fish, bird, and wildlife migration supported
cultural sta-bility. Flexibility and innovation within the social and
governance structures of Indigenous commu-nities supported a robust
knowledge system---hereafter referred to as Traditional Ecological
Knowledge (TEK)---that strengthened capacity to adapt to significant
environmental variability]{.underline} (Barnhardt & Kawagley, 2005;
Pearce, Ford, Wilcox, & Smit, 2014). With the rate of environ-mental
change, the landscape is becoming less familiar to the people that have
always known it well, and TEK is becoming less reliable (Cochran et al.,
2013; Pearce et al., 2014). For example, co-author Huntington recalls a
well-known bear den located near his home community of Huslia. Knowledge
of the den was passed down for gener-ations. It was located on a
thermokarst ridge, which is now the location of wetlands and dry lakes
and the den no longer exists. Within one genera-tion this past knowledge
became obsolete (Huntington & Watson, 2012). While the
Intergov-ernmental Panel on Climate Change asserts that cli-mate change
is one of the most significant chal-lenges facing the globe in the
current century (Larsen et al., 2014), [Indigenous communities will
disproportionately experience the impacts from cli-mate change, due to
not only their strong reliance on the environment for their livelihoods
but also the legacies of colonialism that have challenged the
perpetuation of their way of life]{.underline} (Ford et al., 2018;
McNeely, 2011).

[Despite these challenges, reliance on and shar-ing of fish, wildlife,
and other harvested resources continues to be an integral source of food
security and cultural identity for Indigenous Alaska com-munities.
However, because of current challenges related to climate change,
cultural disruptions, changing economic opportunity, and a complex
management system, there is an increasing need for new creative forms of
learning and knowledge dis-tribution that can effectively support
community-based adaptations. Community-based adaptation to climate
change should be a "community-led pro-cess, based on communities\'
priorities, needs, knowledge, and capacities, which should empower
people to plan for and cope with the impacts of cli-mate
change"]{.underline} (Reid, Mozaharul, Berger, Cannon, Huq, & Milligan,
2009, p. 13). [Relevant commu-nity-based adaptations must consider the
central role that harvest practices continue to play in sup-porting the
livelihoods, health, wellbeing, and cultural identity of Indigenous
communities.]{.underline}

#### Western solutions alone fail---only Indigenous knowledge systems offer place-based strategies capable of sustaining fragile Arctic ecosystems

Simone **Weichenrieder 24** (Simone Weichenrieder, Climate and Nature
Finance Specialist, contributor at The Arctic Institute, co-chair of the
APECS COP29 Arctic delegation, former consultant at the Asian
Development Bank, member of the IUCN World Commission on Protected
Areas, alumna of the Johns Hopkins School of Advanced International
Studies (SAIS) with a dual M.A. in International Economics and
Development Finance. "Leveraging Indigenous Knowledge for Effective
Nature-Based Solutions in the Arctic" The Arctic Institute,
https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/leveraging-indigenous-knowledge-effective-nature-based-solutions-arctic/)

[[The Arctic stands at the forefront of]{.mark} our global [climate
crisis.]{.mark}]{.underline} Experiencing [[warming at more than twice
the rate]{.mark} of [the rest of the planet]{.mark}]{.underline}, the
[Arctic faces]{.mark} dramatic transformations as ancient ice melts,
permafrost thaws, and ecosystems undergo upheaval[. These
[changes]{.mark} lead to a cascade of challenges, from coastal erosion
to shifting wildlife habitats, [threatening]{.mark} both natural
[landscapes and]{.mark} the [communities]{.mark} that depend on
them.]{.underline} In response to these pressing challenges,
[[integrating Indigenous knowledge]{.mark} [into nature-based solutions
(NbS]{.mark}) [presents a]{.mark} promising [approach to]{.mark}
enhancing [climate resilience.]{.mark}]{.underline}

[NbS is a recently emerging term for actions to leverage the power of
ecosystems to protect people, optimize infrastructure]{.underline}, and
ensure a stable and biodiverse future. These natural (climate) solutions
include conservation efforts, habitat restoration, water resource
management, and green infrastructure initiatives.1) By harnessing
natural ecosystems, [NbS can provide up to 37 percent of the mitigation
needed by 2030 to achieve the targets of the Paris Agreement and give
several benefits for climate adaptation and biodiversity
enhancement.2]{.underline}) Informed and well-structured NbS can [reduce
climate change impacts on local communities and counter biodiversity
loss]{.underline}. However, effective [NbS are complex and difficult to
design,]{.underline} [especially in fragile ecosystems]{.underline}.
Therefore, [[Indigenous communities]{.mark} [and their knowledges are
crucial for finding]{.mark} effective [solutions to]{.mark} preserve and
[protect the Arctic]{.mark}.]{.underline}

Combining NbS with Indigenous knowledge, also known as traditional
ecological knowledge, can greatly enhance the effectiveness of local
climate and conservation actions. Indigenous peoples, despite comprising
only a small fraction of the global population, manage between 13 and 20
percent of the world's lands, which contain approximately 80 percent of
the remaining global biodiversity. [[In the Arctic]{.mark}, this
includes communities such as [the Sámi]{.mark} in Finland, Sweden,
Norway, and Northwest Russia[; the Nenets, Khanty, Evenk, and
Chukch]{.mark}i in Russia; [the Aleut, Yupik, and Inuit (Iñupiat) in
Alaska]{.mark}; the Inuit (Inuvialuit) in Canada; and the Inuit
(Kalaallit) in Greenland.3)]{.underline} Since Indigenous communities'
[livelihoods]{.mark} are often [directly linked to the surrounding
land]{.mark}, northern Indigenous groups are particularly vulnerable to
climate change, experiencing its impacts more intensely and rapidly than
many other regions. For millennia, Arctic Indigenous groups like the
Inuit and Sámi have developed sophisticated land management systems and
ecological knowledge crucial for maintaining their ecosystems' delicate
balance.This article explores the importance of combining Indigenous
knowledge with NbS to address climate change impacts in the Arctic,
highlighting the indispensable role of Indigenous expertise in achieving
successful and sustainable outcomes.

[Indigenous knowledge encompasses]{.mark} the deep, [place-based
understanding]{.mark} and practices [developed by Indigenous
communities.]{.mark} This knowledge integrates [cultural]{.mark},
[spiritual,]{.mark} and [ecological insights]{.mark} into managing and
conserving local environments. Indigenous peoples and their knowledge
systems offer holistic and local perspectives on climate risk
management, integrating cultural, intergenerational[, and place-based
understandings in Arctic ecosystems.4]{.mark}) Many Indigenous
communities live on and manage significant portions of the land in
Arctic regions and retain [vital ancestral knowledge and expertise in
conserving nature, protecting biodiversity, and addressing climate and
disaster risks through adaptation and mitigation
strategies.5]{.underline}) In many cases, their connection with the land
and their surrounding ecosystems is deeply rooted in spiritual and
cultural traditions that have evolved over millennia, offering a
distinct perspective on land stewardship that contrasts sharply with
values imposed by Western colonialism.

However, it is essential to recognize the diversity and complexity
within **[Indigenous communities]{.mark}**. While many Indigenous groups
emphasize sustainable practices and environmental stewardship, there are
significant variations in perspectives and approaches. Some [communities
[**support** resource **extraction projects like oil, gas, and mining
for economic benefits**]{.mark}]{.underline}, while others strongly
oppose such activities to protect their lands and cultural heritage.
These differences highlight that Indigenous knowledge systems are not
monolithic but are shaped by distinct cultural, economic, and
environmental contexts[.**Indigenous communities**]{.mark} **[possess
traditional [ecological knowledge essential for
sustainably]{.underline}]{.mark}** [understanding and **[managing Arctic
environments]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Their practices, shaped by an
intimate relationship with the surrounding environment, [emphasizing
long-term sustainability]{.mark}. Most Indigenous governance systems
prioritize community well-being and environmental stewardship, making
them natural allies in climate resilience efforts.6) In this sense,
Indigenous knowledge systems are crucial for safeguarding biodiversity
and ecosystem services.7)

#### And, the Iñupiat in Alaska have already led drilling efforts -- indigenous inclusion improves arctic oil extraction 

Mia **Bennett 21** (Mia Bennett, Associate Professor (Political
Geography & Critical Remote Sensing), Department of Geography,
University of Washington; PhD in Geography from UCLA (NSF Graduate
Research Fellow), MPhil in Polar Studies from Scott Polar Research
Institute, University of Cambridge (Gates Scholar), BA in Political
Science & European Studies from UCLA; former Assistant Professor at
University of Hong Kong, Visiting Fellow at Heidelberg's Centre for
Apocalyptic and Post‑Apocalyptic Studies, and Doris Zimmern
HKU--Cambridge Hughes Hall Fellow at Cambridge. "Can oil development
work for Indigenous Peoples? Views from Borneo and Alaska",
https://www.regionalstudies.org/rsa-blog/can-oil-development-work-for-indigenous-peoples-views-from-borneo-and-alaska/)

On Alaska's North Slope, [many of **the native Iñupiat do favour
drilling** both because they view their traditional practices as able to
coexist alongside industry]{.underline}, and also because the
institutional framework keeps many of the revenues within local
communities. Thanks to ANCSA, a significant proportion of revenues from
oil extraction funnel into Native communities (Huskey, 2018) although
there [are heated debates over the fairness of the distribution of those
revenues. Soon after ANCSA, Iñupiat leaders also pushed to establish
their own municipal government, the North Slope Borough, which is able
to tax the oil industry]{.underline}. In turn, [these taxes subsidize
modern schools and developments like the impressive Iñupiat Heritage
Center in Utqiaġvik, the North Slope's largest settlement]{.underline}.
Each year, whaling crews prepare their boats at the center, which also
hosts permanent displays celebrating the region's rich history. One
individual said to me, "We need development to continue because we don't
want our culture to go extinct."

To some individuals, then[, traditional livelihoods have come to depend
on the oil industry.]{.underline} This idea might seem paradoxical, but
in a way, even though the extraction of fossil fuels causes myriad
negative impacts on the environment and health, ANCSA and the North
Slope Borough's taxation powers have together helped ensure that at
least some of the benefits of extraction stay within the community. Back
in Borneo, when I asked one Penan activist what he thought the biggest
threat to his people's land rights was, he responded, "The law itself."
Its lack of clarity allows regulations to be manipulated to undermine
Penan rights, even customary ones, he elaborated.

Even in Alaska, however, laws that help locals capture the proceeds of
resource development can have pernicious effects[. ANCSA has contributed
to higher social inequality and unsustainable resource development in
places like Southeast Alaska, where the old growth temperate rainforest
has been clear-cut (Dombrowski, 2007)]{.underline}. In my own
encounters, one young man recounted -- with enthusiasm rather than
sadness -- memories of how the oil well where he worked was where his
grandparents had been born many decades ago, when it was just tundra.

In both Borneo and Alaska, development has arrived. Neither the Penan
nor the Iñupiat will return to their nomadic ways. Networks of pipelines
cover the Alaskan tundra, while in Borneo, gridded oil palm plantations
run right up against the rough edges of the dense, tangled jungle.
Still, despite development, the land continues to hold special meaning.
One Penan man described, "To me, the land is much more than the timber.
It's a culture, its music to the soul and it also sort of scenery that
you can draw for yourself. It's a lifeline for the Penan." Ensuring that
the people who live on the land maintain the right to draw the scenery
for themselves, so to speak, and realize the futures they envision --
whether or not development is involved -- critically depends on strong
legal frameworks.

Nevertheless, laws that promote the local accumulation of capital from
resource extraction may enhance support for and hasten the very
processes of development that reconfigure people's relationship to the
land. As Indigenous Peoples gain more rights around the world
(O'Faircheallaigh, 2013), their relationship to the land and the law
will undoubtedly change. [Examining the consequences of these shifts to
the legal and environmental landscapes will require further research
conducted in partnership with local communities, with results compared
across regions]{.underline} (e.g. Haley, 2004). Asia and the Arctic are
far apart, but comparing the experiences of Indigenous Peoples in the
face of global resource development may spotlight successful policies
and practices that can potentially be transferred across continents.

#### Equitable Benefit Sharing rids mining of it's negative effects and positively impacts indigenous communities

**Farmonaut**, 20**25** \[Farmonaut (no specific authors listed), 2025,
Farmonaut, "Indigenous Rights and Mining: 5 Steps for 2025,"
https://farmonaut.com/mining/indigenous-rights-and-mining-5-steps-for-2025#:\~:text=FPIC%20empowers%20Indigenous%20peoples%20to,prior%20to%20any%20resource%20exploitation,"
accessed 7-18 DG\]

["Over 370 million Indigenous people worldwide manage 25% of Earth's
land, yet face 39% of mining-related conflicts."]{.underline}

Introduction: Navigating Indigenous Rights and Mining in 2025

The intersection of Indigenous Rights and Mining remains a critical and
complex issue in 2025. As the global demand for minerals essential to
the green transition---such as lithium, cobalt, nickel, and rare earth
elements---continues to surge, mining projects often unfold within
Indigenous territories. [Here, communities maintain unique cultural,
spiritual, and economic ties to their lands, making the balancing act
between development, environmental stewardship, and respect for
Indigenous sovereignty particularly sensitive.]{.underline}

[Throughout history and into the present, Indigenous Rights and Mining
have raised urgent questions about legal protections, environmental
impacts, socioeconomic opportunities, and the **path to equitable and
sustainable resource management**. The challenge lies in achieving
meaningful **consent**, ensuring that traditional livelihoods are not
disrupted, and that communities are **true partners** in **decisions**
related to their lands.]{.underline}

This in-depth blog will explore the modern landscape of Indigenous
Rights and Mining, examining historical context, legal frameworks,
environmental and social impacts, and actionable steps toward equitable,
sustainable mining practices for 2025 and beyond.

Historical Context and Contemporary Challenges of Indigenous Rights and
Mining

[Mining has historically proceeded with little regard for Indigenous
rights, resulting in widespread displacement, environmental degradation,
and social upheaval.]{.underline}

[Indigenous peoples worldwide have suffered the loss of land, pollution
of water sources, and the breakdown of traditional livelihoods dependent
upon hunting, fishing, and gathering. Despite key international
frameworks such as the United Nations Declaration on the Rights of
Indigenous Peoples (UNDRIP), which emphasizes free, prior, and informed
consent (FPIC), the reality of implementation remains deeply uneven
across regions and countries.]{.underline}

Key challenges remain:

[**Marginalization** of **Indigenous** **voices** in decision-making
processes related to mining permits and operations]{.underline}

Lack of legal recognition and protection of Indigenous land rights

Environmental harm to water, soil, and biodiversity without meaningful
consultation or engagement

Persistent social and economic inequities for many communities despite
resource extraction from their territories

[In 2025, these challenges have become central to both global
sustainability ambitions and ongoing disputes between mining companies,
governments, and Indigenous groups. Navigating the path to equitable and
sustainable practices is fundamental to the future of Indigenous Rights
and Mining.]{.underline}

Legal and Policy Developments in Mining and Indigenous Rights

Increasing global attention in recent years has led to several countries
strengthening legal protections for Indigenous communities in the
context of mining. For instance, Canada and Australia have robustly
incorporated FPIC principles into their regulatory frameworks, requiring
mining companies to consistently engage with Indigenous groups from the
very earliest stages of project development.

Other significant policy evolutions include:

[The adoption of co-management and **benefit-sharing**
**agreements**---transforming Indigenous peoples from **obstacles to
partners** in resource management]{.underline}

A broader international push for corporate accountability, especially as
Environmental, Social, and Governance (ESG) metrics inform investment
decisions

The rise in Indigenous-led organizations asserting their rights using
political and legal tools, including litigation and involvement in
global forums

Increased scrutiny and enforcement mechanisms around responsible mining
practices for sustainable development

[Despite these advances, implementation of FPIC, fair benefit-sharing,
and environmental protections remain uneven, with many communities still
exposed to harm, displacement, and exclusion from meaningful
participation.]{.underline}

Environmental and Socioeconomic Implications of Mining in Indigenous
Territories

The environmental impacts of mining operations in Indigenous territories
are profound. These range from:

Deforestation and habitat destruction

Soil erosion, introducing instability and affecting traditional land
uses

Contamination of critical water sources, impacting villages and urban
settlements downstream

Loss of biodiversity and disruption of ecological stewardship held by
Indigenous peoples

Degradation of lands with spiritual and cultural significance

[At the same time, responsible mining that respects Indigenous rights
and implements robust environmental protections can deliver real
socioeconomic opportunities:]{.underline}

[Quality employment opportunities and training for local
communities]{.underline}

[Investment in infrastructure, education, and healthcare]{.underline}

[Equitable revenue-sharing that supports cultural resilience and
economic independence]{.underline}

[Empowerment of traditional knowledge in land management]{.underline}

[The balance between harm and opportunity, degradation and sustainable
management, is **shaped** by whether **Indigenous voices are at the
heart of decision-making processes**---with FPIC and strong legal
enforcement as critical levers.]{.underline}

"By 2025, [sustainable mining could reduce environmental impact by up to
30% in Indigenous territories with equitable practices."]{.underline}

The Path Forward: 5 Principles for Equitable Mining Practices

[To address the critical intersection of Indigenous Rights and Mining in
2025, we outline five guiding principles for equitable and sustainable
mining practices. These steps draw from international best practices,
legal frameworks, and lessons learned from decades of mining in
Indigenous territories.]{.underline}

[1. Recognition of Land Rights]{.underline}

[Ensuring that Indigenous land tenure and title are legally recognized
is foundational to protecting communities from involuntary displacement
and resource exploitation without consent.]{.underline}

[Legal recognition of lands safeguards against forced relocation and
exploitation]{.underline}

[Land rights serve as the cornerstone for economic security and cultural
survival within Indigenous communities]{.underline}

[Policy reforms must close gaps in ownership claims and clarify
jurisdiction over mining projects]{.underline}

Modern technology, like Farmonaut's real-time satellite monitoring
(large-scale resource management tools), can help governments and
Indigenous groups verify land use, deter illegal operations, and support
enforcement of land rights.

[2. Free, Prior, and Informed Consent (FPIC)]{.underline}

[FPIC empowers Indigenous communities with the right to approve or
reject mining projects before work begins, based on transparent and
accessible information.]{.underline}

[**Consultation** must be ongoing, **culturally appropriate**, and in
languages that communities understand]{.underline}

[Consent cannot be tokenistic; participation must be meaningful and free
from intimidation or coercion]{.underline}

[Robust regulatory frameworks are needed to guarantee FPIC
implementation and address violations]{.underline}

[3. Environmental Protection and Traditional Knowledge]{.underline}

[**Integrating Indigenous environmental knowledge** with scientific
assessments **enhances the stewardship of lnds, water, and
biodiversity.**]{.underline}

[Indigenous **stewardship** practices, based on generations of
traditional knowledge, promote **restoration**, **biodiversity**, and
holistic resource management]{.underline}

[Collaborative **monitoring** and adaptive **management** strategies can
**minimize** **ecological** **harm**]{.underline}

[4. Benefit Sharing and Capacity Building]{.underline}

[**Mining**, when approached **equitably**, can help Indigenous
communities unlock **economic** **development** while **supporting**
cultural **priorities**.]{.underline}

[Mutually agreed benefit-sharing agreements distribute value fairly
(e.g., revenue, employment, local infrastructure)]{.underline}

[Capacity building---via education, training, and technology
transfer---**ensures communities can fully benefit and steward mining
outcomes**]{.underline}

[Investments in community capacity foster decision-making power over
both short- and long-term development]{.underline}

5\. Legal Enforcement and Corporate Accountability

Effective legal enforcement and transparent corporate behavior are
critical to preventing violations of Indigenous rights and environmental
standards.

Strengthened enforcement mechanisms ensure perpetrators of harm are held
accountable and that agreements are honored

Corporate transparency and strong ESG governance drive improved
practices in response to investor and consumer expectations

Tools like Farmonaut's blockchain-based traceability (traceability
solutions) provide a secure and auditable record of resource flows

Satellite, AI, and Tech Solutions for Sustainable Mining in Indigenous
Territories

The pathway to sustainable and equitable mining is strengthened by the
latest technology solutions---a space in which we at Farmonaut are
dedicated to empowering governments, companies, and Indigenous
communities. Harnessing satellite imagery, AI analytics, and blockchain
traceability, the modern mining sector can:

Monitor land use and environmental impact in real time, ensuring prompt
action to prevent or remediate ecological harm

Boost transparency, giving all stakeholders access to actionable data
regarding mining operations, emissions, or resource movement

Provide independent, verifiable traceability of mineral resources from
extraction to market, addressing concerns about conflict minerals and
illegal mining

Support governance and legal enforcement by documenting compliance with
FPIC protocols, benefit-sharing, and ESG standards

Empower Indigenous communities with tools to manage resources and
participate in land use planning and economic development

Our solutions are designed for accessibility across web, Android, iOS
apps and API integration. Developers can find API details in our
Developer Documentation.

These innovations avoid the need for expensive hardware on-site, further
democratizing access to high-quality monitoring and enabling responsible
governance in the face of 2025's mining challenges.

Moving Forward: Equitable Resource Development for 2025 and Beyond

As the global economy pivots towards a future underpinned by critical
minerals for clean energy and technology---lithium, cobalt, nickel, rare
earth elements---the relationship between Indigenous Rights and Mining
only grows in significance.

Key takeaways for the present and future include:

[Recognizing and upholding Indigenous sovereignty is
indispensable---both for justice, and for aligning with international
legal expectations.]{.underline}

[**Genuine**, early **engagement** with Indigenous communities creates
the **foundation for equitable benefit-sharing and sustainable
development.**]{.underline}

[Environmental sustainability can be achieved by marrying traditional
knowledge with scientific and technological tools.]{.underline}

Corporate accountability and legal enforcement are crucial for raising
standards and ensuring community well-being.

[By following these principles and leveraging tech-driven insights, the
mining sector can promote responsible resource development, fuel
socio-economic opportunity, and minimize harm across Indigenous
territories. Industries, governments, and communities that prioritize
these guidelines will be better positioned to navigate the complexities
and demands of 2025 and beyond.]{.underline}

What are Indigenous Rights in the context of mining?

Indigenous Rights refer to internationally recognized legal principles
that protect the sovereignty, land, culture, and economic opportunities
of Indigenous communities. In mining, this includes the right to free,
prior, and informed consent (FPIC) over resource extraction, as well as
participation in economic benefits and environmental protection.

Why is FPIC important in mining projects involving Indigenous
territories?

FPIC empowers Indigenous peoples to meaningfully decide on mining
projects that may affect their lands and livelihoods. It ensures they
have the right to accept or refuse a project, based on full information,
without coercion, and prior to any resource exploitation.

How does mining affect Indigenous communities environmentally and
socially?

Mining can lead to ecosystem degradation, pollution, and loss of
traditional livelihoods if not properly managed. It may also bring
positive outcomes like employment and infrastructure when practices are
equitable and communities are partners in decision making.

What legal frameworks protect Indigenous Rights in mining?

Key legal frameworks include the United Nations Declaration on the
Rights of Indigenous Peoples (UNDRIP), national constitutions in
countries like Canada and Australia, and increasingly, regulatory
agencies that enforce FPIC and benefit-sharing agreements.

How can technology help achieve equitable mining practices?

Technology like satellite monitoring, AI analytics, and blockchain
traceability enhance transparency, accountability, and environmental
oversight, empowering all stakeholders to make informed, equitable
decisions and to verify responsible mining operations.

How do Indigenous communities benefit from sustainable mining?

[If respected, sustainable mining can bring investment, training,
employment, and infrastructure to Indigenous communities, helping to
strengthen economic autonomy and support cultural preservation, while
ensuring environmental stewardship.]{.underline}

Conclusion: Charting a Responsible and Equitable Path for Indigenous
Rights and Mining

The nexus of Indigenous Rights and Mining remains critical in 2025. [The
challenges and opportunities at this intersection demand a
collaborative, sustainable, and just approach]{.underline}---[one that
centers Indigenous sovereignty and leverages legal, technological, and
ethical principles for positive impact.]{.underline}

Navigating the path ahead will require unwavering respect, transparency,
and robust enforcement alongside new tools and frameworks. By embracing
these standards, we can transform the future of mining---[ensuring that
it serves as an engine not just for economic growth, but for the
resilience and prosperity of Indigenous communities
globally.]{.underline}

### AT: Tribes Love the Environment\-\--2AC

#### Their logic homogenizes tribal interests and weaponizes them to further their Western environmentalist agenda. 

Daniel **Raimi &** Alana **Davicino 24**, Fellow at Resources for the
Future, Lecturer at the University of Michigan Gerald R. Ford School of
Public Policy, M.P.P. from the Duke University Sanford School of Public
Policy; Budget Analyst at the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency,
M.P.A. from the Indiana University O\'Neill School of Public and
Environmental Affairs, "Securing energy sovereignty: A review of key
barriers and opportunities for energy-producing Native nations in the
United States," Energy Research & Social Science, Vol. 107, January
2024, accessed 07-21-2025, https://doi.org/10.1016/j.erss.2023.103324,
DG

[Despite the **homogenizing assumptions**]{.underline} that are often
[projected onto Native Americans]{.underline} (e.g., [that indigenous
peoples\' values **always align** with the modern]{.underline} Western
[environmental movement]{.underline}), [scholars]{.underline} across
disciplines have [identified]{.underline} and explored [the **wide range
of perspectives** that Native nations and individuals hold on energy
development]{.underline}. Like any other community or group, divergent
views may arise because of differing priorities, perspectives, and
because any given project will have different distributional
consequences \[26,35--37\].

For instance, [scholars]{.underline} have [explored]{.underline} the
[cultural and social reasons for Diné coal workers\' support for
maintaining]{.underline} coal plant [operations, despite the
consequences to the]{.underline} local landscape, water resources,
public health, and the [climate]{.underline} \[35\]. In a series of
interviews, another analysis documented the wide range of concerns,
along with perceived economic opportunities, described by Diné peoples
related to energy development \[38\]. In the MHA Nation, leaders have
adopted the slogan "sovereignty by the barrel," reflecting the goal of
advancing tribal sovereignty through wealth gained from oil and gas
extraction on the reservation \[39\].

[One very high-profile symbol of these differing views is]{.underline}
the Dakota Access Pipeline ([DAPL]{.underline}), [through which 60 % of
the oil produced on the Fort Berthold reservation traveled in
2020]{.underline} \[40\]. DAPL garnered national media attention due to
the efforts of [activists from the Standing Rock Sioux
Tribe]{.underline}, who [opposed the pipeline]{.underline} largely due
to concerns over risks to drinking water \[41\]. [By contrast, **leaders
from the MHA Nation voiced support**]{.underline} for the pipeline\'s
operations, [citing]{.underline} the **[economic
consequences]{.underline}** of the pipeline being shut down \[40\].2
Views on DAPL likely vary considerably within the MHA nation, but this
example demonstrates how leaders across different tribes may disagree on
issues of energy development.

[Another]{.underline} example [comes from the Chaco Canyon area, a
center of pre-Columbian Pueblo culture]{.underline} for hundreds of
years that is now partly protected and managed by the federal Bureau of
Land Management as Chaco Culture National Park. [Oil and gas development
has occurred]{.underline} in the surrounding region [for
decades]{.underline}, but the Biden Administration has halted new
drilling within a 10-mile radius of the national park to protect
cultural resources \[43\]. Although this proposal has been supported by
some Native nations in the region \[44\], [the Navajo nation has argued
for a 5-mile radius to allow its members to produce]{.underline} more of
their [oil and gas]{.underline} resources [for the economic benefits
that development would provide]{.underline} \[45\].

[Energy development]{.underline} also [has **uneven effects** within
Native nations, and will lead to different perspectives within
tribes]{.underline}. For example, [the Bakken oil boom created
disparities between MHA members who signed lucrative oil and gas leases,
those who signed leases]{.underline} at [below market value, and those
who did not sign leases at all]{.underline} \[39,46\]. This diversity of
perspectives highlights the value of tribes making their own decisions
about energy, as outside entities may not understand the complex
dynamics within a tribe.

Indeed, [mining companies, governments, and environmental organizations
have]{.underline} at times [sought to use energy development and the
controversies it creates to **further their own interests**, regardless
of whether those]{.underline} interests [are well-aligned with
tribes]{.underline} \[26\]. Numerous analyses have also pointed out that
public perceptions of Native Americans, whether representative or not,
are used by stakeholders to advance their own narratives \[26,47\].

## Framing\-\--2AC

### Extinction K\-\--2AC

#### Impact calculus that centering on 'extinction first' reinscribes whiteness and legitimizes genocidal settler and imperial state violence

Audra **Michell &** Aadita **Chaudhury 2020** (Audra Mitchell, CIGI
Chair in Global Governance and Ethics, Balsillie School of International
Affairs, and Associate Professor at Wilfrid Laurier University, former
Senior Lecturer in International Relations, department of Politics,

Aadita Chaudhury, Ph.D. Candidate in Science and Technology Studies,

to]{.mark} ongoing [disaster]{.mark}. Throughout, we focus on how

[[Discourses that predict]{.mark} the imminent '[end of]{.mark} the
[world']{.mark} are [not as universal as they]{.mark} often
[claim]{.mark} to be. The futures they fear for, seek to protect and
work to construct are rooted in a particular set of global social

**[segregate bodies through the inscription of racial

[accrues benefits]{.mark} -- including material, physical, and other
