### Perm\-\--2AC

#### perm do both. western modes of thinking of land are being appropriated to break down settler colonial practices

Paul Berne **Burow et al**, 20**18** \[Paul Berne Burow is a social and
environmental scientist at Stanford University, academic background is
situated in environmental anthropology, Indigenous environmental
studies/sciences, human-environment geography, and ethnoecology, Samara
Brock received her PhD at the School of Forestry and Environmental
Studies at Yale University, Michael R. Dove Margaret K. Musser Professor
of Social Ecology at Yale University; Professor of Anthropology, Curator
of Anthropology Peabody Museum; Co-Coordinator, Combined
YSE/Anthropology Doctoral Program, 2018, "Unsettling the Land:
Indigeneity, Ontology, and Hybridity in Settler Colonialism,"
Environment and Society, https://www.jstor.org/stable/26879578?seq=1,
accessed 7-18-2025 // ava :)\]

Indigenous Ontology: Hybridity and Revitalizing Relationships with Land

A case study of the Confederated Salish and Kootenai Tribes (CSKT) of
the Flathead Indian Reservation, located in the northwestern United
States, can illustrate how hybrid ontologies of land offer ways to
consolidate a tribal land base in the service of revitalizing
relationships with the other-than-human world. [Much of the CSKT's work
is oriented toward reversing the dispossession of Indigenous lands by
Euro-American settlers who claimed title to newly created homesteads in
the early twentieth century]{.underline} (Bigart and Woodcock 1996).
This was made possible by the transformation of land into property,
which took place through US government--led land surveys and subsequent
allotment (Latta 1989). Despite the territorial-bounded reservation of
land stipulated in the 1855 Treaty of Hellgate, more than half of the
unceded land within the territory of the Confederated Salish & Kootenai
Nation was sold as property to white settlers (Smith 1979). It was not
until 1934 that the US federal allotment policy ended, but that did
nothing to reverse the prior loss of land. Only in the wake of the civil
rights and Red Power movements did governance policies begin to change
in the 1970s (Ruppel 2008). This marked the beginning of the era of
revitalization. [Today, the officials at the CSKT Tribal Lands
Department have a clear sense of their objective: to return land to
tribal control, but not strictly as a form of property.]{.underline}
This land base also provides for the expansion of land-based practices
such as caring for culturally important relations with the nonhuman
world, the protection of landscapes imbued with collective memories, and
the provision of material benefits to the Native community, such as
housing.

The CSKT Tribal Lands Department was established in 1982. It emerged
from the Tribal Realty Office that was created in the 1960s, with Bureau
of Indian Affairs realty staff tasked with overseeing the titling and
transfer of common and individual trust lands on the reservation. The
new Tribal Lands Department grew to take on additional activities,
including permitting surveys, land use planning, and, most importantly,
land acquisition. The 2000s saw the increasing size and role of tribal
governance in land acquisition. This came with the emergence of new
technologies, like geographic information systems (GIS), as tools to
track, manage, and prioritize its landholdings starting in the 1990s.8
Lands were purchased at the market rate and then put through a process
to have the land transferred to trust status, meaning it became tribal
land again (though still as property). Still, [the consolidation of
tribal landholdings facilitates the creation of spaces for practices
that recognize and instantiate relationships among the human and
other-than-human.]{.underline}

[The emergence of the Tribal Lands Department marks a shift in which
bureaucratic institutions of land management, many of them prescribed by
federal rules, came to dominate how land was used and therefore
perceived. As discussed earlier, these bureaucratized modes of managing
land carry their own subjectivities, which embed their own ontologies of
land. [Settler state institutions of private property cannot be entirely
circumvented given the extant relationship between tribal nations and
the US government, but relationships can be revitalized even within
these institutions.]{.mark} In this context, it is also hard to imagine
revitalization without attention to alternate modalities like the
relationality described by many scholars]{.underline} (Blaser 2016;
Carroll 2015; Coulthard 2014; TallBear 2017; Todd 2015). [[If land is
constituted in the ways it is governed, then it is also tied up with
different practices, from]{.mark} the spatial subjectivities of GIS to
[manage land and target its reacquisition, to the subjectivities of land
as constituted in the reciprocal obligations of tribal land use
practices.]{.mark} These different subjectivities are oriented toward
different affordances of land. Spatial technologies are amenable to
registering the value of "resources" such as timber but not to the more
phenomenological idea of an experience relating to other-than-human
persons---or stories that represent and reproduce collective memories
constitutive of Indigenous belonging to place.]{.underline}

[[Land and]{.mark} the [environment are always bound up in the
historical discursive apparatuses of the state---not just settler
states, but also Indigenous states---at the same time as Indigenous
peoples are successfully contesting these logics and
practices]{.mark}]{.underline}, a central point made by Carroll (2015).
[But [working to reacquire land solely on]{.mark} the register of
[settler state regimes of land ownership and capitalist relations is
limiting]{.mark}, so [land-based practices are essential to reworking
the]{.mark} very [notion of land underpinning settler colonial
domination.]{.mark} [Settler modes of thinking about the land are being
appropriated to take the land back while other modes of thinking are
being practiced.]{.mark} This story is not limited to bureaucratic
processes of land acquisition and management: it also involves
land-based practices that focus on reciprocal obligations and the
other-than-human to revitalize alternate ontologies. Land-based
practices, in many cases, occur outside the purview of tribal government
departments. But they are connected, as funding to support cultural
activities frequently comes from the tribal government, although
cultural leadership largely emanates from a council of elders. This
highlights a tension that exists in Indigenous governance in which
formal state institutions contend with practices that are underwritten
by very different ontological categories. Sometimes this emerges as
conflict over the disposition of land. The work of [decolonization
operates at]{.mark} this [nexus where different modalities of land
coexist, often]{.mark} uneasily or [in conflict with each other.]{.mark}
Therefore, [decolonization cannot simply be]{.mark} the [rejection of
one modality of land for the other, but rather follows an uneasy path to
maintain a hybrid assemblage of ontologies]{.mark}, with all their
attendant affordances and limitations.]{.underline}

### AT: Ontology K\-\--2AC

Second card here is a great way to straight turn the K if you are brave
and debate with gusto.

#### Reducing Indigenous ontologies to a single approach limits their power by mythologizing Indigenous agency. 

Jenny **Pickerill 24**, Professor of Environmental Geography at the
University of Sheffield, Ph.D. in Geography from the University of
Newcastle upon Tyne, "Unsettling Geography: Enacting the Politics of
Indigenous Ontologies," Agoriad: A Journal of Spatial Theory, Vol. 1,
No. 1, 2024, accessed 07-21-2025, https://doi.org/10.18573/agoriad.16,
DG

[Indigenous ontologies are **multiple, place-specific, fluid,
irreducible, and complex**]{.underline} (Whyte 2018; McGregor 2018).
**[They cannot be reduced to a technical]{.underline}** or artefactual
**[additions to dominant approaches, nor can they be employed as an
additional perspective that resolves the limitations of]{.underline}**
Anglo- Eurocentric and [**colonially shaped geographies**. If ontologies
are merely considered as forms of]{.underline}
'[difference]{.underline}' [they are easy to dismiss, assimilate or
reduce to]{.underline} their own **[mythic unities]{.underline}** (Reid
& Sieber 2020). [Seeking to extract elements from Indigenous
ontologies]{.underline}, for example, [to rethink]{.underline} the
[culture-nature dualism, without attending to the deep political
implications of Indigeneity, **reduces and confines its possibilities
and power**]{.underline}. This [selective engagement is]{.underline}
also [a form of **appropriation** and **colonisation of Indigenous
knowledge**]{.underline}. It is vital, therefore, that geographers
engage with Indigenous ontologies in conceptual, political, and
practical ways.

This [engagement with Indigenous ontologies should be]{.underline}
further [complicated by acknowledging]{.underline} that [many
people]{.underline} already [navigate **multiple
ontologies**]{.underline} (**[it is not a dualistic framework of
Indigenous versus Anglo-Eurocentric ontologies]{.underline}**). This
does not mean, however, that it is necessarily possible nor indeed
desirable to seek to translate or equate between different ontologies.
de la Cadena (2010) argues that plural worlds are not commensurable --
[we must live with the acknowledgement of pluriversality and **resist**
the desire to]{.underline} '[resolve]{.underline}' [differences between
ontologies into a singular way of understanding the world]{.underline}.
In other words, [engaging with Indigenous ontologies is not about fully
translating, knowing, and understanding everything]{.underline} about
these ontologies; [rather, the political point is]{.underline} for
geographers [to acknowledge that **we live in a world of plural
ontologies**, the tensions between and navigation of which offer us
insight into the possibility of living differently, better understanding
what it means to be human, and multispecies justice]{.underline} (Hunt
2014; Pellow 2016). [Central to this is a need to **work with the
unknowable**]{.underline} (that outsiders can never fully know
Indigenous ontologies) [while advocating for political changes that
**support the right of Indigenous ontologies to persist and have
material presence** in the world. This advocacy may involve]{.underline}
forms of '[co-becoming]{.underline}' (Bawaka Country et al. 2016),
[self-determination, or **asserting**]{.underline} land or [**resource
ownership privileges**. This clashes with]{.underline} some
anthropological [approaches which seek to verify the detail and veracity
of Indigenous ontologies]{.underline} in order [to construct a universal
understanding of humanity]{.underline} (see Viveiros de Castro's 1998
discussion of the concept of Amerindian perspectivism).

As geographers have long explored how knowing about the world is
embodied and materialised in ways of being, and consequently expressed
through practices, we are ideally positioned to work with Indigenous
ontologies productively. As Blaser (2014) argues, [ontologies are a way
of worlding, but it is only in how they become enacted, practised, and
performed that they become political]{.underline} (see also Clément's
2017 examination of embodied Māori ontologies). It is in this enactment
that geographers can (and already are) politically advocating for change
in the academy and the world. It is in this political enactment,
therefore, that Indigenous ontologies are unsettling the academic
discipline of geography.

Using the praxis of 'unsettling' disciplinary thought (building on
Meehan et al. 2023), this paper examines how relational geographies are
unsettled by Indigenous ontologies, particularly in acknowledging the
agency of place, that everything cannot become knowable, and the
inseparability of epistemologies from ontologies. Relationality works
best when it is generated through what Barker and I have conceptualised
as 'doings together in place' (Barker & Pickerill 2020) -- engaged,
careful, empirical research through practices and lived experiences that
unsettle assumptions about what it is possible to know, and instead
builds relations through 'doings'. Doing geography differently generates
transformative practices that challenge the coloniality of our
university institutions, advocate for and support hopeful alternatives,
work for self-determination and promote multispecies justice. [Central
to this]{.underline} [process is engaging in the **specifics** of place,
practices and lived experiences, and how as researchers we must get
embroiled in the complex]{.underline} '[doings]{.underline}' [of
Indigenous ontologies]{.underline}. I conclude by reflecting on the
possibilities this unsettling enables, while being cautious of the risks
of engaging with Indigenous ontologies in ongoing colonial, extractive,
and violent ways.

#### Trying to create a singular ontology [reproduces]{.underline} Western power relations, and valorizing 'Indigenous knowledge' denies its specificity.

Jenny **Pickerill 24**, Professor of Environmental Geography at the
University of Sheffield, Ph.D. in Geography from the University of
Newcastle upon Tyne, "Unsettling Geography: Enacting the Politics of
Indigenous Ontologies," Agoriad: A Journal of Spatial Theory, Vol. 1,
No. 1, 2024, accessed 07-21-2025, https://doi.org/10.18573/agoriad.16,
DG

Second, [Indigenous ontologies unsettle the idea]{.underline} that
[everything can become knowable. There is a **danger in seeking to
abstract specific knowledges into universalised
understandings**]{.underline} (of concepts like place) of **[overwriting
the specificity of Indigenous ontologies]{.underline}**. As Bawaka
Country et al. (2016) argue, there are limits to human perceptions such
that we cannot perceive or necessarily understand all the co-becomings
that are occurring, but we must trust that they are there because we may
be impacted by them in some way. [Accepting]{.underline} this
[unknowability respects the **fluidity of Indigenous knowledge**. Such
knowledge is **not a fixed entity** which can be consumed and turned
into a product for academic knowledge production; it is a **living,
connecting reference point** and]{.underline} '[the future of Indigenous
rights and political struggles **depend** on the ability of Indigenous
knowledge to retain its active, mobile, relational nature **rather than
the fixity**]{.underline} it is given in colonial law, **[stuck at the
point of contact with colonizers]{.underline}**' (Hunt 2014: 30).

As Viveiros de Castro (1998) and de la Cadena (2010) advocate,
[respecting the]{.underline} place-based [**specificities** of
Indigenous ontologies, and not seeking to equate between]{.underline}
the [ideas, symbols, or objects in these knowledges]{.underline} with
those in Anglo-Eurocentric geographies [is **crucial politically**.
There can be **no certainty, verifiability, or necessarily clarity of
knowledge for Anglo-Eurocentric scholars**]{.underline} of Indigenous
ontologies, [and **seeking simple translations can confuse** rather than
aid **understanding**]{.underline}. Similarly, [Indigenous ontologies
cannot easily be appropriated or aligned for political
gain]{.underline}. de la Cadena (2010) demonstrates this in examining
the differences between environmental arguments against a mine with
Indigenous activists' claims. [While sometimes]{.underline} these
[political groups might align]{.underline} (even with different
rationales), [they can just as easily end up in opposition because
of]{.underline} the [different relationalities]{.underline} of
Indigenous ontologies. [The danger here]{.underline}, then, [is in
**reducing Indigenous ontologies to a politically useful position,
rather than engaging with their ongoing, emerging, and dynamic
complexity**]{.underline}. Likewise, the full extent of this complexity
cannot be known by Anglo-Eurocentric geographers, and instead must be
trusted as an unknown.

Accepting unknowability leads to two further unsettling realisations:
the consequences of plural ontologies and pluriversality, and that
relationalities can have purposeful boundaries. [The notion
that]{.underline} we live in plural worlds -- that [there are plural
ontologies and many people already navigate multiple
ontologies]{.underline} -- [is increasingly accepted]{.underline} by
human geographers (Larsen and Johnson 2012a, 2012b, 2016) and as Noxolo
argues '[decolonialisation is a process of building towards the
**pluriversality of knowledge**]{.underline}' (Noxolo 2017: 318). This
multiplicity makes sense of geographical assemblages and ethnographic
identification of 'others' (Blaser 2014). However, this recognition
troubles attempts at identifying commonalities that might be necessary
in seeking politically progressive action and in determining political
agency (Ioris 2020). Pluriversality and unknowability complicate
attempts to build common political agencies at scale. At the same time,
while in theory 'everything is always in relationship with everything
else' (Kanngiser et al. 2024: 1), and therefore there are ongoing
processes of transformation and mutation through these relations, in
practice politically different ontologies will be articulated as
separate to assert political goals, such as political representation or
land back (Bawaka Country et al. 2016). There are two processes at play
here: that relational thinking is articulated as bounded and bordered
for political gain, but also that the lived experiences of Indigenous
ontologies are of one that is placed, material, limited and in
juxtaposition to, often Anglo-Eurocentric ontologies of settler
colonialism. As Cochrane and Arredondo (2005) argue, 'relational
thinking implies openness that often belies the lived-experience of
many' (cited in Bawaka Country et al. 2016: 460). [There are political
advantages to articulating boundedness and this distinctiveness can
reflect a lived experience of contestations between
ontologies]{.underline}.

Finally, **[Indigenous ontologies cannot be considered separately from
epistemologies]{.underline}** -- ontologies are best understood through
practice and doings (Wilson 2020). [Indigenous ways of knowing are
expressed and articulated as ways of being and doing]{.underline}, 'we
become tangible proof of our ontology ... we are able to show (Do),
respectfully and rightfully (Being) what we know (Knowing)' (Martin &
Mirraboopa 2003: 210). [Indigenous knowledges]{.underline} centralise
relationality in epistemological processes, and through embodied,
phenomenological, and affective approaches to knowledge production. This
**[shapes how research is conducted just as much as it shapes what
research is seeking to understand]{.underline}**.

[Relationalities]{.underline}, therefore, [are central to working with
Indigenous ontologies, but also unsettle Anglo Eurocentric
methodological and ethical demands]{.underline}. Indigenous scholars
require autonomy to develop and work with, and through, Indigenous
epistemologies with accountability to Indigenous communities and
protocols first and foremost. [Indigenous research]{.underline}
obviously [requires]{.underline} collaboration and [interaction with
participants in non- extractive ways that are **always in
process**]{.underline} -- in relation -- **[and need to remain
open-ended and sustained over time]{.underline}**. Crucially, these
[relational engagements]{.underline} also [enable the **refusal** and
**rejection of involvement in academic research**. This refusal of
co-becoming is a vital aspect of resurgent Indigenous nationhood
which]{.underline} means **[sometimes it is better not to do the
research at all]{.underline}**.

[Indigenous ontologies]{.underline}, therefore, [unsettle Anglo
Eurocentric approaches to relationalities by **focusing on the agency of
place**, challenging the notion that everything can become knowable, by
demonstrating that relationalities can have purposeful political and
lived boundaries, and in the inseparability of epistemologies from
ontologies]{.underline}. The [relationalities]{.underline} (and
non-relationalities) [of Indigenous ontologies **disrupt attempts at
universalism** and the appropriation of Indigenous knowledges for
colonial gain]{.underline}. Furthermore, [there is a need to value and
respect the **distinctiveness** of]{.underline} Indigenous [place-based
ontologies given]{.underline} how [they are explicitly tied into
political calls for the return of Indigenous lands]{.underline}. It is
also vital to acknowledge that [land rights here are **about much more
than questions of ownership, but underpin language, culture, and
self-determination**]{.underline}.

### Decol Not a Metaphor\-\--2AC

#### Focusing on anything except for [material demands]{.underline} for divesting colonial power reproduces their impacts. 

Gabrielle **Legault &** Denica **Bleau 25**, Assistant Professor of
Indigenous Studies at the University of British Columbia Okanagan, Ph.D.
from the University of British Columbia Okanagan; Public Scholar and
Ph.D. candidate in Interdisciplinary Studies at the University of
British Columbia Okanagan, "Indigenizing or Appropriating? Navigating
the Boundaries of Institutional Decolonization," Capitalism Nature
Socialism, Vol. 36, No. 2, 2025, accessed 07-21-2025,
https://doi.org/10.1080/10455752.2024.2445586, DG

On the contrary, [decolonization is]{.underline} understood as
"[reclaiming and restoring]{.underline}" a [pre-colonial means of doing,
being and acting]{.underline}, or "taking away the colonial" (Brant
2023). [Decolonization is **not the simple recognition of
wrongs**]{.underline} done through colonialism, [but]{.underline} the
**[actual and intentional actions]{.underline}** and choices [made to
allow and create means by which Indigenous ways of knowing, being, and
doing are restored]{.underline} (Indigenous Corporate Training 2017).
Linda Tuhiwai Smith, a Māori researcher and scholar, argues that the
process of [decolonization is not a quick process, but requires the
enduring progressions of]{.underline} "the [bureaucratic, cultural,
linguistic and psychological **divesting of colonial
power**]{.underline}" (Smith 2012, 101). Through this process, there are
systemic and ontological struggles from [colonial powers]{.underline},
who [**must participate in action** and reject]{.underline} [hegemonic
and imperialistic practices and teachings, while encouraging the
**revitalization of Indigenous knowledges**]{.underline} (Smith 2012).
Eve Tuck, a Unangax scholar, writer and poet, and K. Wayne Yang, a
settler scholar, state that **[decolonization as a
metaphor]{.underline}** "**[is a premature attempt at
reconciliation]{.underline}**" (2012, 9). However, [when decolonization
is employed properly and respectfully]{.underline}, "[is not an 'and.'
**It is an elsewhere**]{.underline}" (Tuck and Yang 2012, 36). This
further implies that true [decolonization **affirms** full **Indigenous
sovereignty over decision-making**]{.underline} (Brant 2023; Tuck and
Yang 2012).

### AT: Land Acknowledgement\-\--2AC

#### Land acknowledgements are [empty gestures]{.underline} that actively trade off with [material decolonization]{.underline}. 

Gabrielle **Legault &** Denica **Bleau 25**, Assistant Professor of
Indigenous Studies at the University of British Columbia Okanagan, Ph.D.
from the University of British Columbia Okanagan; Public Scholar and
Ph.D. candidate in Interdisciplinary Studies at the University of
British Columbia Okanagan, "Indigenizing or Appropriating? Navigating
the Boundaries of Institutional Decolonization," Capitalism Nature
Socialism, Vol. 36, No. 2, 2025, accessed 07-21-2025,
https://doi.org/10.1080/10455752.2024.2445586, DG

Enter "[decolonization]{.underline}", **[a buzzword like those before
it]{.underline}**: "inclusivity", "diversity", "reconciliation" (or was
that after? It's hard to keep track). Post-secondary [institutions
jumped at the opportunity to declare their commitments to
decolonization]{.underline}, but Indigenous scholars and communities
affirm that institutions continue to replicate acts of research harm on
studied communities, while perpetuating unsafe classrooms and work
environments for Indigenous peoples. [Land acknowledgements, intended to
unsettle settler presence and benefit from stolen Lands, **have become
customary** throughout institutions]{.underline} over the last decade,
[**yet, the recognition of ongoing theft without meaningful action or
change has been** critiqued as **performative**]{.underline} (Lambert,
Sobo, and Lambert 2021; Robinson et al. 2019).

Decolonizing and Indigenizing within a Colonial Academic Institution

In both teaching and research, [the upheaval of decolonization **does
not require**]{.underline} mere **[recognition of Indigenous Lands but
aims to center Indigenous ontologies, epistemologies, axiologies and
pedagogies]{.underline}** -- rooted in ancestral and historical
knowledge, teachings, and laws. Smith (2012, 41) writes,
"[decolonization]{.underline}, however, **[does not]{.underline}** mean
**[and has not meant a total rejection of all theory or research or
Western knowledge]{.underline}**. Rather, [it is about centering our
concerns and world views and]{.underline} then [coming to know and
understand theory and research]{.underline} from our own perspectives
and for our own purposes." For indigenization to occur, it is necessary
for relationships to be established, and for Indigenous peoples to
instruct this process. Here we see decolonization and indigenization
used interchangeably, as calls to critique the existing privileging of
western knowledge systems, while making space for Indigenous ways of
being and knowing, "embedding Indigenous practices, ideas, and
principles into our academic pursuits" (Pete 2016, 81). [To attempt
either decolonization or indigenization is inherently
political]{.underline}, and to the western eye, such institutional
renovation can be perceived as overly biased or subjective. We know.
That's the point.

Elsewhere, I've critiqued the role that "well-intentioned professors,
administrators, and even self-ordained scholar-activists have in
'[metaphorizing]{.underline}' [decolonization in lieu of engaging in
**practical** and **pragmatic efforts** that concretely promote
Indigenous sovereignty, land rights, and
self-determination]{.underline}" (Gahman and Legault 2019, 2). Such
efforts, **[like the performative]{.underline}**- only [**Land
acknowledgement, defeat the intent and purpose of decolonization**,
which is to not only recognize the existence of Indigenous peoples,
Lands, and knowledges but also affirm and promote their validity and
rights]{.underline}. Within academia, it is important that we are
intentional in the ways that we confront colonial entities while staying
true to our ontologies and epistemologies. Through engaging in the
Kelowna Urban Indigenous Youth Project together as Principal
Investigator (PI) and Research Assistant (RA), we sought to be mindful
of the ways in which we were engaging in decolonization and/or
indigenization.

#### They exclude by inclusion. 

Jerry **Coyne 21**, Professor Emeritus of Ecology and Evolution at the
University of Chicago, Ph.D. from Harvard University, "The uselessness
of land acknowledgments," Why Evolution is True, 04-07-2021,
https://whyevolutionistrue.com/2021/04/07/the-uselessness-of-land-acknowledgments

But there are [problems with]{.underline} these
[acknowledgements]{.underline}. First, as I noted above, they don't deal
with successive occupation of the land:

> . . . the [statement from University of Texas names no fewer than ten
> tribes before concluding]{.underline} the sentence [with]{.underline}
> an embarrassed "[etcetera]{.underline}," which acknowledges "all the
> \[other\] American Indian and Indigenous Peoples and communities who
> have been or have become a part of these lands". The truth of the
> matter is that [any piece of land]{.underline} in the modern-day
> United States [was likely held by various native tribes over the
> course of the Pre-Columbian era and the early American
> republic]{.underline}. In other words, **[we can't even be sure who
> needs to be]{.underline}** "**[acknowledged]{.underline}**" for the
> land: much of the [**info**rmation is lost to history]{.underline}.

And, more important, Ellwanger emphasizes that [many Native Americans
did not share our]{.underline} capitalistic [preoccupation
with]{.underline} "[owning]{.underline}" [land]{.underline}. He gives
several examples; here's one:

> Massasoit Sachem (leader of the Wampanoag confederacy) is reputed to
> have asked "What is this you call property? It cannot be the earth,
> for the land is our mother, nourishing all her children, beasts,
> birds, fish and all men. The woods, the streams, everything on it
> belongs to everybody and is for the use of all."

And he adds this:

> Thus, [by]{.underline} "[acknowledging]{.underline}" [the native
> claims to a piece of land and implying]{.underline} that
> [these]{.underline} claims [supersede and negate the
> claim]{.underline} that [modern]{.underline} local and federal
> [governments make]{.underline} upon the territory, the [Land
> Acknowledgement Statements **erase the**]{.underline} very
> **[particularities of Native American cultures]{.underline}** that
> these [academics purport to honor and preserve]{.underline}. In short,
> the non-Native academics speak on behalf of the people whose dignity
> they claim to uphold: by appropriating the right of those people to
> speak, they inadvertently inflict the very sort of cultural violence
> that they profess to abhor.

This all makes sense, but of course even if Native Americans didn't have
our concept of "property", they were still displaced from their lands by
settlers. To me that seems just as bad, especially when they were
forcibly driven to desolate reservations. I don't know the solution to
this, except to say that [Native Americans continually displaced each
other by the **same methods**]{.underline} (war, broken treaties, and so
on), and we are just part of that history.

To me, [land acknowledgments are the **height of performative
wokeness**: statements that **accomplish absolutely nothing** save to
call attention to your heightened consciousness]{.underline}---and
perhaps impart a history lesson, but why is that part of a talk or a
syllabus? [If you're on stolen land, then **give it back instead of
moaning about it**. It's as if one began a class by saying "I'm using a
laser pointer I stole from Professor Jones, but I'm not going to give it
back to him."]{.underline}

#### It's a reverse voting issue. 

Jeffrey Ian **Ross 22**, Professor in the School of Criminal Justice,
College of Public Affairs at the University of Baltimore, Ph.D. in
Political Science from the University of Colorado Boulder, "What's wrong
with Native American land acknowledgements at university commencement
and graduation exercises?", Jeffrey Ian Ross, 12-12-2022,
https://jeffreyianross.com/whats-wrong-with-native-american-land-acknowledgements-at-university-commencement-and-graduation-exercises

Although I'm not the only person to have difficulty with public native
land acknowledgements, I think that the college/university context or
platform deserves greater scrutiny.

First, [Native American land claims acknowledgements are part of a
continuum that includes the commodification, co-optation,
fetishization]{.underline}, etc.[of important social
relationships]{.underline}.

Second, I would argue that [the audience in attendance **don't** really
**care**]{.underline}. If it's the graduating students, then [their
major goal]{.underline} is to get their degree, pose for pictures with
their fellow students, family, and maybe a faculty member or two if they
are in attendance. If it's the parents or loved ones of the graduating
students, then it is to celebrate the day. And [if it's the staff,
faculty and administrators it's to **get the hell out of there** as
quick as possible]{.underline}.

Third, [public Native American land claims acknowledgements
**disingenuous** and **disrespectful**]{.underline}. Why? If we are
talking about universities, then rarely does this mean that the
institution of higher education has a native American studies program,
or department, [It does not mean]{.underline} that they will be hiring
more American Indian faculty, or [making a concentrated
effort]{.underline} to recruit American Indian students more.

Fourth, one must conclude that [issuing land acknowledgements
is]{.underline} mostly [**performative**. It's a way to show to those in
attendance]{.underline}, and perhaps a larger audience [that the
university]{.underline} cares or **[can check the box]{.underline}**.

Fifth, [the issuance of land claims acknowledgement is **hypocritical**.
If]{.underline}, for example, [the university]{.underline} where the
land claims acknowledgement is being made [dates back to
pre-emancipation times, one can]{.underline} almost entirely
[assume]{.underline} that [parts]{.underline} of the university [were
built with slave labor]{.underline}. But as one can predict [this is
**rarely acknowledged**]{.underline} in these public spectacles.

Sixth, [public land claims statements like this is **faux activism** and
**virtue signaling**]{.underline}. Their ability to change things is
really limited.

In short, just like many public displays, Native American [land claim
acknowledgements **don't really do anything to help** native peoples
**or repair the wrongs that have been done**]{.underline}.
